M.Sc. Thesis
Master of Science in Engineering
Investigation and design of stabilizing balloon-
frame walls and structural details in medium
rise mass timber buildings
Scott B. Dimmock
Kongens Lyngby 2021
==PAGE:2==
DTU Civil Engineering
Department of Civil Engineering

Brovej

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Demand for efficient and sustainable construction has lead to development in timber struc-
tures and especially cross laminated timber, CLT. The relatively new construction material
is still in its early development, although it has been implemented in several projects world-
wide. The majority of the projects that have endured the task of designing with CLT have
been residential housing, where the many separating walls allows for alternative load paths.
This is usually not the case for commercial buildings, where flexibility and large open areas
are part of the general design. As a result, the stabilizing system is often being located in
the centre of the structure accommodating elevators and restrooms. This variation in design
leads to challenges for the stabilizing system in terms of load transfer of vertical and hor-
izontal forces. This study will investigate if the proposed layout of a 5 storey commercial
building, designed by British architect firm Waugh Thisthleton, is structurally possible using
only mass timber for the superstructure. This also includes the question as to how it may be
accomplished and what structural details that are needed.
The critical stabilizing walls of the 5 storey building have been subjected to a combination
of loads in order to verify capacity and design the critical connections for the bracing sys-
tem. Analytical and Numerical modelling have been used to determine the deformations
and stresses of the wall elements and the connections that join them. In addition, the base
hold down connection have gone through an experimental test campaign to investigate the
characteristic capacity, stiffness properties and future potential use.
The findings of the investigation showed that CLT panels have great in-plane strength and
can accommodate large in-plane loads applied to them, especially if the walls are oriented
vertically spanning several storeys at the time, so-called balloon framing. This method elim-
inates the issues associated with compression perpendicular to the grain, the weak direction,
which normally limits the ability to build taller. The design of the base hold down connec-
tion that was experimentally tested showed significant strength and stiffness properties and
therefore has grate potential for several connection designs, base hold downs in particular.
On the basis of this study, it has been shown that it is possible to build larger commercial
office buildings using only mass timber for the superstructure, which offers advantages in
construction efficiency and costs.

CHAPTER1
Introduction
Wood is a natural and renewable material, and the most significant building material used
today that is grown by the sun. When harvested responsibly, wood is arguably one of the best
tools architects and engineers have for reducing green-house-emissions and storing carbon
[1]. In the last two decades the interest and development within the field of mass timber
have increased significantly and by the end of this decade the production of Cross Laminated
Timber (CLT) is expected to proceed 1.000.000 cubic metres [2].
A number of precedent low- to high-rise wood buildings have been constructed in the last
decade, including hybrid wood-concrete structures. The low-rise structures have primarily
been build using a honeycomb or modular structure/system enabling the loads to have multi-
ple paths. This is usually not the case for commercial buildings where flexibility in use leads
to open spaces, often resulting in the stabilizing system being located wherever fitted. This
introduces a higher magnitude of load concentration to the bracing system and the interface
joints.
This project branches from the Build-In-Wood (BIW) project work package 2. Build-In-
wood is a European funded Horizon 2020 project with the aim of reducing green-house-
gas emissions from the construction sector through encouraging and facilitating increased
adoption of timber products within Europe and the World [3]. Work package 2 is responsible
for the design and technical development of building systems for multi-storey wood buildings
between 5 and 10 storeys. The aim is to identify best practices and optimised solutions based
on available technologies and products [4]. Despite being a branch of BIW this project has
not received any funding.
Figure 1.1 Precedent mass timber projects; a) 25 King street (Australia), b) Sky Believe in better (London),
c) T3 building (Minneapolis USA), d) Kajstaden Tower (Sweden), e) Brock Commons (Vancouver),
f) Mjøsa Tower (Norway) g) TREET (Norway)
==PAGE:14==
1 Introduction
Waugh Thistleton Architects have, as part of the Build-In-wood project, designed the lay-out
of a 5 storey commercial office building which functions as the starting point for this work.
The architectural design is result of thorough investigation of possible structural systems,
(honeycomb, modular, post and beam) which would best suit the projects aim, and allowing
for incorporation of modularised elements within the system. The Floor plan is presented in
chapter 2.
Although CLT is being investigated for use in structures of up to 20 and 30 storeys, this
project will focus on a more typical structure of five storeys which fits within the the majority
of structures at a global level. The findings and design concepts of this project are scaleable
and suitable for mass timber structures in general.
This project will focus on the structural design of the critical bracing walls for the five storey
building, and the investigation and design of the connections in the bracing system applying
the state-of-the-art knowledge and European Standards.
1.1
Object and aim
The objective of this study is to determine whether the given layout of the proposed 5 storey
building is possible to achieve using only mass timber for superstructure, within reasonable
dimensions. This also involves the design of the connections needed to join the elements and
material interfaces. For the connections to be successfully implemented they have to fulfill
performance and safety criteria as well as the ease of manufacturing and installation.
Having the superstructure consisting of only mass timber enables faster construction due to
the installation process and off site manufacturing. Additionally, clashes between material
interfaces like wood and concrete are reduced as the tolerances can be made tighter and only
one crew is needed at the job site. Mass timber offers a more quiet construction phase com-
pared to steel and concrete which is appreciated in urban environments. The construction
process only requires simple tools to install and shape the wood elements. There exists sev-
eral products within mass timber, where CLT will be the main consideration of this project.
The limitations of wood structures are in most cases related to the interfaces and how these
are joined together. When designing these structures the question arises as to how the loads
are to be transferred to the foundation while maintaining the required performance. The
design of bespoke connections are usually part of in-house knowledge, which is not com-
monly shared. This study aims to build on to this body of knowledge, by presenting the
state-of-the-art research.
The architectural drawings of the 5 storey building function as design basis. Although the
design is performed for a structure situated in Denmark, the results in general and the designs
presented are principal and can be adapted elsewhere.
The ultimate goal is to contribute to the development of sustainable construction and develop
design solutions that the industry will incorporate in future projects or use as reference for
development of their product portfolio.
2
==PAGE:15==
1.2 Methodology
1.2
Methodology
Market research has been used to establish a set of options for the different structural de-
tails. The most promising of the alternatives were then designed and calculated for each
case. The resulting solutions were evaluated in terms of resource efficiency, cost and level
of complexity, with emphasis on the ease of construction and simple manufacturing. The
comparative evaluation enabled the identification of which solutions that showed to be the
most promising with the highest performance.
When analyzing the structure and the structural details, both analytical and numerical meth-
ods were used. The numerical investigations are performed to provide results for complex
problems, while the analytical methods function as the theoretical basis for comparison in
addition to the design verifications. Applied theory is based on the available state-of-the-art
research, and literature.
Numerical analysis is performed using the Finite Element (FE) software FEM-Design by
Strusoft. The software allows for modelling and computation of CLT shell structures using
its built-in CLT module. FEM-Design can also provide design verification based the Eu-
rocodes and available literature. The software enables analysis of complex problems which
would be challenging or near impossible by hand calculations. Numerical analysis is in gen-
eral performed as shown by the flowchart, see Figure 1.2 [5]. It is the engineers task and
responsibility to define the problem and the post processing of the results e.g. stresses and
deformations. The results are generally to be verified by means of analytical methods.
Engineer task
Define the 
problem
Discertisation
Define element 
stiffness matrix
Assemble global 
stiffness matrix
Solve
Validation
Post-processing
Software task
Figure 1.2 FEA flowchart [5]
The project is organized like structural design is normally carried out. This implies the def-
inition of the problem, material parameters, geometry, load assumptions and finally, design
of structural details. Parts of this process are iterative in that the design of the connections
effect the load progression. Therefore, there might be deviations from this logical structure
based on iterations that have been performed in the background.
Limitations
Due to the time-scope of this project topics like cyclic loading, earthquake design and fire
performance have not been covered and may be subject to future work.
1.3
Design ethos
The following list describes the characteristics that have been evaluated in decision making
when investigating different alternatives.
• Maximize use of timber
3
==PAGE:16==
1 Introduction
• Maximize adaptability
• Dry solutions
• Minimize complexity in installation/construction
• Minimize complexity in manufacturing
• Material efficiency
• Maximize component repetition /minimize overall number of components and configurations
For the design to be successfully implemented most of these criteria are required to be ful-
filled.

==PAGE:17==
CHAPTER2
Description of project
The project uses the design proposal developed by WTA to investigate and design the load
bearing bracing system and the critical connections within this system. The building of
interest is a 5 storey commercial office building. A post and beam system was determine
based on the flexibility it provides, principally shown in Figure 2.1. The bracing system and
structural details had not been investigated and is subject to this project.
Figure 2.1 Post and beam structure
A brief list of some the primary decisions that were made by WTA relevant for the this
project are listed below.
• Use of post and beam structural system to achieve maximum adaptability
• Range of spatial requirements depending on building used defined
• Service strategy defined
• Core configuration, location and structural function defined
• Baseline grid for different building sizes agreed
• Baseline floor build-up, based on use of dry, demountable products defined.
• Multiple connection design approaceds for most problematic areas (post and beam) defined
• Approach to fire-safety design: fully/partially exposed structure in commercial buildings.
Although WTA and their partners have determined the general structural system and some
of these connections. There were still question as to how the lateral loads were to be trans-
ferred to the foundation. The floor plan is shown in Figure 2.4. A number of space dividing
walls were already present, which are also used for bracing optimizes material use. Other
alternatives are to brace the exterior walls, which being effective introduces more joints,
structural details and challenges. The initial idea is therefore to use the walls along the el-
evators, staircases and shafts. Even if the thickness of these walls has to be increased, the
amount of labour is more or less the same.
==PAGE:18==
2 Description of project
Two types of framing methods are common, balloon type and platform type, see Figure 2.2.
The first being named after a common residential construction practice in North America in
the late 19th century. Its characteristic is that the walls run continuously along the height, and
the floor structure butts into the wall. Platform type is characterized by its name by that each
floor functions as a platform for the next. The key difference is that platform construction
have interrupter wall segments bearing on the weakest direction of the floor plate. This
is normally not an issue for smaller house complexes where the number of dividing walls
introduce small concentrated forces due to the many load paths of such structures.
a)
b)
Figure 2.2 a) Balloon frame, b) platform frame
It is first when the forces increase due to the floor plan or building height that the platform
type has its limitations. Two main considerations govern this limitation. I) Reduced strength
perpendicular to the grain of the CLT slabs, II) Cumulative elastic compression and shrink-
age, see Figure 2.3. Other factors are e.g. the number of connections needed for each ele-
vation, resulting in many connections and therefore greater deflection and the limited storey
height due to orientation of the panels in transport.
Balloon Frame
Platform Frame
Figure 2.3 Cumulative shrink and elastic compression for two frame systems[1]
Because of these limitations it was decided early on that a balloon frame type structure would
be the most viable option for this case. Due to the large ”cantilevers” at either end in plan
view, it was decided with the architect that the door openings are moved from axes 3 and 8
to axes C and D. This allows the end walls to be continuous, which is needed to avoid too
high force concentrations for the remaining walls in that direction.

The main panels are brought to a height of around 1m above the third floor due to the moment
zero of a continuous wall and the ease of installation for the next segment. The primary
connections that dictate if the solution is feasible/reasonable or not, are D1 (BHD) and D2
vertical joint. The joint D5 can be created as a small scale version of D1 given that the
magnitude of loads decrease with height.

==PAGE:21==
CHAPTER3
Theoretical background
This chapter presents the main theoretical considerations on which the investigations for this
project were based.
3.1
Mass Timber/ Definitions
Mass Timber is a collective designation of large format engineered wood products, some
of which are Cross Laminated timber (CLT), Glued laminated timber (Glulam), Laminated
strand Lumber (LSL), Laminated veneer lumber (LVL), Parallel Strand Lumber (PSL), Ribbed
CLT, and more. Examples of these are shown in Figure 3.1. The abbreviations usually refer
to the characteristics or manufacturing method.
Laminated Veneer Lumber
(LVL)
Laminated Strand Lumber
(LSL)
Glued Laminated Timber
(Glulam)
Cross Laminated Timber
(CLT)
Parallel Strand Lumber
(PSL)
Figure 3.1 Examples of large format engineered wood products
All the before mentioned products with the exception of Glulam are produced in large panels.
Although the strength and elastic properties vary depending on manufacturing method and
grade of lumber, all the Mass Timber products may be used for plate or wall bearing tasks
with similar performance. In this project Cross Laminated timber is the primary material of
investigation due to its rapid demand and high performance.
3.2
Cross laminated Timber
Cross Laminated Timber is an engineered plate shaped wood product composed of a number
of layers - mostly odd numbered, where each layer is consist of lamellae. The layers are
usually glued orthogonal to one another with the exception of some outer layers running
in parallel. The crossing layers give the element great in-plane and out-of-plane strength
enabling it to be used for plate and/or wall applications. The cross lamination also provides
stable elements with little to negligible movement under moisture variation in the elements
plane. Elements, or panels, mat be manufactured with or without edge gluing of the narrow
side of the lamellae. Panels of CLT can be manufactured up to 5m wide and 30m long with
thickness up to 500mm. The limitations often arise from transport where any panel wider
than approximately 3m require special care.

3.3
Material properties
Cross laminated timber is usually manufactured from C24 grade timber or similar, indicating
a compressive strength of 24MPa. CLT like Glulam is composed of glued lamellae which
evens out the gross section material quality. This enables the material to have overall better
strength and elastic characteristic compared to that of the single lamella. The strength can
be calculated or determined by testing, which is performed in accordance with EN 16351 -
Cross laminated timber.
3.3.1
Material properties of the lamellae
The strength and elastic properties of the individual boards or lamella are based on the stan-
dard EN 338- Structural Timber. When referring to the lamellae in the following the addi-
tional subscript l is used e.g. fc.0.k is referred to as fc.0.l.k, in accordance with notation in EN
16351. In future reference the subscript l refers to the individual timber boards structural
properties.
3.3.2
Properties of the CLT element
The elastic properties of the gross CLT element is treated analogous to glued laminated
timber, ref. EN 14080. The elastic modulus along grain E0 is determined by
E0 = 1.05E0.l
(3.1)
10

as the timbers shear modulus Gl.
G = Gl
(3.2)
The characteristic elastic values (5% fractiles) for the CLT element are determined by
E0.05 = 5/6·1.05E0.l = 5/6·E0
(3.3)
G0.05 = 5/6·Gl
(3.4)
where E0 is the before mentioned along grain E-modulus and Gl the shear modulus of the
timber. In some instances the characteristic values may be referred to as E0.05 or G0.05 refer-
ring to the 5% quantile values. The rolling shear modulus Grol can be estimated to be 1/10
the shear modulus
Grol = 1/10·Gl

ρk = 1.1·ρk.l

3.3.3
Strength properties of CLT
Some additional strength parameters are defined especially for CLT as the glued interfaces
and multiple layers introduce additional behaviour which needs to be addressed and verified.
Three shear failure mechanisms (FM) are distinguished. Mechanism I ”net shear”, Mecha-
nism II, ”torsional shear” and Mechanism III ”gross shear” [6]. The three mechanisms and
their characteristic strengths are briefly presented, while the theory is presented in section 3.4
.
Shear strength of individual boards (net), Mechanism I
For in-plane loading, Mechanism I ”net-shear” corresponds to shearing perpendicular to
the grain of the net cross section in the controlling plane [6]. The cross-laminations provid
additional reinforcement resulting in test values of C24 grade lumber up to 10MPa, however
a characteristic value (5% fractile) fv.xy.k = 5.5MPa was proposed for lamellae with thickness
tl ≤40mm.
Torsional strength, Mechanism II
When the elements are loaded in-plane, the glued faces of the boards are subject to torsional
stresses, which may cause failure to the glued interfaces. Numerous investigations have been
conducted on the topic [6] leading to a strength parameter fv.tor.k = 2.5MPa, which is also
proposed in Wallner-Novak et al. (2018) [7].
Gross shear, mechanism III
Gross shear failure will normally occur parallel to grain in all layers. This requires the layers
to be edge glued, with the absence of cracks, allowing shear stresses to be transferred between
the lamellae in the same layer. Missing or insufficient connection between the lamellae at
the edges disables the shear transfere in that direction [6]. For the resistance against gross
shear failure Flaig and Blaß (2013) suggested to use a value fv,k = 4.0MPa for CLT made of
C24 grade timber according to EN 338. It is however conservative to assess CLT as equal
to Glulam with respect to shear strength and therefore adopting the value of fv.k = 3.5MPa.
Rolling shear strength
Loading perpendicular to the grain direction can cause rolling shear failure, see Figure 3.5.
The rolling shear stiffness is noticeably lower than the along grain shear stiffness (approx-
imately 1/10) which effects the resistance to deformation when the plate is loaded out-of-
plane. This effect has to be considered when looking at the out-of-plane plate stiffness as it
reduces the overall stiffness compared to solid timber.

3.4
In-plane loaded CLT elements
CLT elements loaded in-plane have to be verified against shear failure. Three principle shear
failure mechanisms are distinguished.
• mechanism I net-shear
• mechanism II torsional shear
• mechanism III gross-shear
If the boards are edge glued and there is absence of cracks, mechanism I and II will not occur
as the element will behave as a single panel. In practice, there might be cracks that then will
lead to non-glued element behaviour [8].
Mechanism I ”net-shear” corresponds to shearing perpendicular to the grain of the net cross
section in the controlling plane, while Mechanism III is associated with shearing parallel to
grain of the whole CLT element [6]. If the boards are not edge glued, the shear force has to
be transferred between the boards layers through torsion in the boards wide face resulting in
torsional stresses, mechanism II.
13
==PAGE:26==
3 Theoretical background
Two methods for analysing the internal stresses in the CLT element are distinguished. The
method for infinitely thick panels with equal lamellae thickness and the method for pan-
els with varying lamellae thickness. Here the method for infinitely thick panels with equal
lamellae thickness will be presented with the transformation to panels with finite thickness
and equal thickness lamellae. The two methods yield the same result if the thickness of
the lamellae used in the CLT element are the same. For CLT panels with varying lamellae
thickness see Bogensperger, Moosbruger and Silly (2010).
RVE
RVSE

Figure 3.7 Figure showing the Representative Volume Element (RVE) and Representative Volume Sub-
Element (RVE) in relation to the CL element
Following the notation of Bogensperger et al. [9] a Representative Volume Element (RVE)
is introduced. The RVE element is equal in thickness to the CLT element, consisting of
lamellae with equal thickness tl, and has a width and height equal to the board width a.
The RVE has infinitely many layers N −→∞, all with the same properties. The RVE can
be further simplified to a Representative Volume Sub-Element (RVSE). The sub element is
equal in width and height to the RVE with a thickness tl composed of half the thickness of
two orthogonal boards in a node as shown in 3.7 [6]. The RVSE represents the smallest unit
at an intersection between two orthogonal boards with a stress state that describes the global
behaviour of the CLT element [10].

corresponds to failure mechanism III, which requires intact edge bonding in all layers and
the absence of cracks. Missing or insufficient edge bonding disables the transfer of shear
stresses which then have to be carried by the net section and the torsion in the board’s wide
face. The net shear stress τnet,d is determined by
τnet,d = 2·τ0,d

by the net section in the controlling plane, see Figure 3.8, .

3.5
Overturning of stabilising walls
The stabilising walls often referred to as shear walls shall be verified against overturning
when subjected to lateral loads, see Figure 3.10. Stabilising walls are naturally stable as
principally shown in Figure 3.9. The wall represented by the circle will tend towards the
lowest energy level when subjected to a change in rotation, i.e. overturning.
F
θ
Figure 3.9 Principal sketch of stability configuration for shear walls subjected to a lateral load F. The
sudden change in equilibrium occurs when the limit rotation θ is reached, leading to the wall
tipping over its toe
The favourable vertical load N is often not sufficient in resisting the lateral loads, especially
when the wall is slender and tall which may be the case for multi-storey CLT structures.
Therefore, if the self weight is insufficient, additional anchoring is required. The wall must
also be verified for the axial compressive stress from the reaction in addition to the in-plane
stresses.

3.6 Deformation of wall plates / wall diaphragms
The wall is required to be anchored if the eccentricity eM has a magnitude larger than half
the width of the wall, eM ≥b/2. The eccentricity is determined from
eM = M
N
(3.11)
where M is the overturning moment calculated by M = F ·h, and N is the axial load. In the
case of eM < b/2 the reaction is within the body of the wall and anchoring is not needed.
The stress can be assumed to have an elastic or plastic distribution. The elastic distribution
in combination with anchoring is challenging to compute and is therefore estimated with FE
software.
To be on the safe side, the stress distribution can be assumed to be plastic (rectangular) over
the width wy, given that wy ≤b/4 ref. [8]. The plastic stress block must of course be able to
obtain the compressive force. The anchor force can be estimated by taking moments around
RC provided that a reasonable width of wy is assumed. The expression becomes
RT = M −N ·
( b
2 −wy
2
)
(
b−wy
2 −ez
)
(3.12)
in which M is the overturning moment, N the axial force, wy the width of the stress block
and ez the eccentricity to the anchor. The compressive force RC is determined by considering
equilibrium in the vertical direction
RC = N +T
(3.13)
The width of the stress block can be taken as wy = b/4. The magnitude of the compressive
stress should not exceed the compressive strength nor the buckling capacity unless more
detailed analysis is performed.
3.6
Deformation of wall plates / wall diaphragms
The in-plane deformations of the wall plate has to be within acceptable limits. It is not
obvious what the particular limits are as CLT is not covered by the current provision of the
Eurocode. Different litterature sources report that the deflection of a multi storey wall should
not surpass the deflection limits H/400 to H/500, while individual walls may be up to H/300
ref. [11] [8]. In general there are four contributions to the deformation of CLT walls, for
which can be summed up in the elastic range using superposition. These four deformation
contributions are:
• Shear deformation, uv
• Bending deformation, um
• Rigid body Rocking of the wall, ut
• Rigid body sliding of the wall, us
17

strate and brackets, when using multiple. Simplified, the lateral deformation in the top of
the wall can be estimated by the sum of the contributions from
utot = uv +um +ut +us
(3.14)

In the case where the anchor force is known, the actual value replace the simplification. Since

significant and may be the primary deformation source. For this reason the stiffness of the
connections is key in order to achieve a sufficient performance level.
Sliding displacement of the wall element
Sliding deformation is the result of slip in the shear connection. This deformation contribu-
tion depends on whether the walls friction resistance in the compressed zone is governing.
A conservative assumption is to say that once the wall starts moving the friction resistance
becomes negligible and all deformation is obtained in the horizontal connection. The lateral
deformation can be determined by
us = F
kh
(3.19)
where kh is the lateral stiffness per unit length. For multiple connections along the length
the load is distributed accordingly.
The deformation contributions presented so far have all been for a simple wall element with
a single force in the top. The methods can be expanded for multi storey balloon frames
using superposition where the forces and heights are arranged in e.g. vectors. The total
deformation is then simply the sum of the contributions at each level.
Coupling of walls, which have not yet been discussed, may contribute significantly to the
walls deformation. This especially holds true when the vertical joint between the panels have
low shear stiffness leading to individual panel behaviour. This is discussed and analyzed in
more detail in 3.7.
3.7
Coupling of walls
A fifth deformation contribution can be obtained in segmented shear walls, where the to-
tal wall comprises of a number of segments. The kinematic behaviour of connected wall
segments depends on i.e. the joint stiffness and geometry.

3 Theoretical background
3.8
Finite element formulation
FEM-Design from Strusoft was the chosen FEA software this project. This is predominantly
due to the authors previous experience with the software, and that the software has incorpo-
rated CLT as one of its modules in the recent years. CLT panels are modeled using laminated
composite shell theory.
Laminated Composite Shell theory
The theory of Laminated Composite Shells are well documented in Strusofts manual [10].
For the readers convenience a brief overview is presented in the following. The laminated
composite shells in FEM-Design applies the first-order shear deformation shell theory (FSDT)
also called Reissner-Mindlin theory [10]. The shear contribution under deflection of moder-
ately thick shells are not negligible for wood, especially laminated shells. The shear correc-
tion factors applied to the transverse shear stiffness plays an important role to get appropriate
deformation and stress results, which for CLT indicate the rolling shear effect.
Due to its composition of orthogonal layers, the material behaviour of cross laminated tim-
ber is orthotropic. The force-deformation behaviour in the two main directions are therefore
different.The Voight notation is used, 1 = x, 2 = y, 3 = z, in addition to the so-called engi-
neering strain vector. The matrix is built up in the same way as Strusoft presents it.
Bending and twisting
Out-of-plane shear
In-plane (membrane) actions
Figure 3.14 Internal forces in 2D structural elements, source [14]
In the case of general laminated composite shells the constitutive shell stiffness matrix will
be much more complicated than the isotropic case [10]. The so-called constitutive shell
stiffness matrix for Reissner-Mindlin shells are not that simple if the material model is not
isotropic [10]. To calculate this type of shells a homogenization method along the thickness
of the shell is required. The method is sometimes referred to the Equivalent Single Layer
Method (ESLM) in some literature. The shell constitutive matrix, Eq.(3.29) is the result of
the homogenized general laminated composite shell method. As it can be seen, the general
laminated composite shell is more complicated due to the layers having their own orthotropy

The reductions are only applicable to CLT panels with symmetrical layers due to the conse-

The reduction values r33 = 0.65 is suggested in the swedish CLT handbook due tp the pre-

transverse shear stiffness as this is automaticly calculated by the software leading to a value
closer to κ = 1/4 rather than the well known 5/6. The effective in-plane shear stiffness may
be reduced by a factor of r66 = 0.75 according to Bogensperger et al. (2010) [9].
Non-linear uplift calculation
When numerically evaluating the overturning of stabilising walls a non-linear elastic uplift
calculation is performed. It is possible to define defined point, line and surface supports that
only take compression in FEM-Design. This problem with non-linearity is solved through
an iterative process. The relation between the reaction forces F and displacements e can be
interpreted by the diagram in Figure 3.16.
+e
+F
Figure 3.16 Diagram principally showing the non-linear elastic calculation in FEM-Design
The solution implemented in FEM-Design checks, in iterative steps, if there exists tensile
forces in the connection points. If tensile forces are present the linear static analysis is re-
peated through applying a low stiffness value in the tension element. The process is repeated
until there are no tension in the supports.
24
==PAGE:37==
3.9 Load bearing walls
3.9
Load bearing walls
The initial phase of determining the statical system and possible wall configurations rely on
the size and capacity of the walls. The swedisch CLT handbook [15] proposed three wall di-
agrams which gives approximate dimensions for a combination of in-plane and out-of-plane
design loads. The diagrams are limited to relatively thin walls and the configuration of the
wall lamellae are not stated. To build upon this knowledge a set of additional capacity dia-
grams have been developed for wall sizes applicable to low and medium rise buildings. The
diagrams are based on CLT wall lamellae arrangements which can be found in the industry.
As there is no obvious buckling check in the eurocode for CLT panels, the capacity have
been determined based on available theory and literature. The method follows the Eurocode
methodology for buckling verification based on the effective length and reduction factor of
the compressive strength, with the key difference that it also accounts for the shear flexibility
of the member which ultimately lowers the axial capacity.
3.9.1
Buckling of walls
The load carrying capacity of walls is mostly determined by buckling. The buckling be-
haviour of members are govern by the ratio of cross sectional area to stiffness against de-
formation of the member [7]. The stiffness against deformation comprises of a portion of
bending stiffness and shear stiffness, which for CLT members can not be neglected. Includ-
ing the portion of shear stiffness reduces the critical buckling load. The buckling behaviour
is normally assumed to follow a sinusoidal or cylindrical shape. This shape occurs if the
wall is constrained to move laterally at the top and bottom and are uniformly stressed by the
vertical load and/or moment [7]. For instances where vertical edges are constrained or the
compressive stress is locally high next to areas of low compressive stress the behaviour may
result in a spatial bulging. This behaviour requires special attention and is not covered here.
For buckling analysis, Wallner-Novak et al. (2018) [7] proposed two methods that accounts
for the influence of shear stiffness via a factor kcs, which increases the slenderness of the
member. The factor is applicable to the two models - the extended gamma method or the
shear flexible beam according to Timoshenko.
Gamma method
kcs =

The further calculation procedure is similar to EC5 where the compression strength is re-

==PAGE:38==
3 Theoretical background
3.9.2
Laminated Composite Shell theory
The theory used to determine the capacity of the walls is the so-called Laminated Composite
Shell theory (LCSt). The theory, being applicable to verifying the buckling behaviour in
multiple directions and account for membrane effects, have been reduced to the simplest
case of a vertically along grain loaded wall member. The stiffness formulation reduces to
that of a shear flexible beam, as there are no membrane effects for the given configuration.
The theory is analogous to a shear flexible beam with the exception of the shell formulation.
The method follows the Eurocode methodology (3.34) which combines the compressive and
bending stresses with the reduction factor kc for relative slenderness λrel > 0.3.
σc,0,d
kc · fc,0,d
+ σm,0,d
fm,0,d
≤1.0
(3.34)
The shear factor methods presented by Wallner-Novak et al. (2018) have been compared
to the Laminated Composit Shell theory, in which the outcome of the investigation showed
that the capacity was slightly higher for the shear factor methods than for LCSt. For that
reason the design diagrams have been developed based on the theory that provides the most
conservative result, i.e. the lowest capacity.
The elastic properties of the CLT walls have been chosen according to Table 3.4 with a minor
variation. Strusoft deals with the characteristic properties through the use of a reduction
factor for the properties of the lamellae. The reductiuon factor is in this case 9240/11000 =
0.84, which is why there is a deviation compared to the values in Table 3.4. This deviation
ultimately lowers the capacity with a negligible amount. The system factor ksys have been
set to 1.0 for all cases.

3.9.3
Discussion
In common for the methods presented, the shear stiffness makes a significant capacity re-
duction and can not be ignored. Both the extended gamma method and shear flexible beam
method considers this effect, however, it was found that both methods predicted a slightly
higher capacity compared to the composite shell theory method under the investigation of a
140 140mm 5 layer CLT wall. Further analysis of other wall thicknesses or lay-ups were not
investigated. The variation was small, in the magnitude of a few percent, and may therefore
be neglected.
In terms of buckling, a parabolic moment curve is more critical than the moment curve
obtained from eccentric loading by e.g. a corbel. Eurocode 5 does not differ between these
cases as the utilization is based on simple stress utilization from axial and bending stresses.
It is therefore up to the interpretation of the user to which buckling length that should be
applied.
31
==PAGE:44==
3 Theoretical background
3.10
Joints with inclined screws and steel plates as outer
members
The load-carrying capacity of connections with dowel-type fasteners and steel plates as outer
members of a single shear connection, determined on the basis of Johansen’s yield theory,
is limited by the embedment strength of the timber member and the bending capacity of the
fastener [16]. The use of screws perpendicular to the grain direction is inefficient because of
the limited diameter and low bending capacity.
The application of inclined screws with steel plates as outer members arranged at 45◦be-
tween screw axis and member was first presented by Krenn & Schickhofer (2009) [16]. The
work was based on previous publications made by Betkja & and Blass and Kevarinmäki in
2002. They showed that the load carrying capacity and stiffness of inclined screws in timber-
timber connections was significantly higher compared to single shear connections due to the
high withdrawal capacity of threaded screws.
Krenn & Schickhofer investigated the use of thick steel plates as outer members, which had
special holes capturing the inclination and geometry of the screw. This novel approach has
its economic advantages in the connections easy application, while the designer benefits
from the increased capacity and stiffness. Originally, thicker steel plates with special hole
geometry were initially used, which have been further developed to allow for use of thinner
steel plates with specialized 45◦washers as seen in Figure 3.24. This further reduces material
consumption and manufacturing time, as the holes needed are simple slots to allow for the
seating of the washer.
Figure 3.24 Inclined screws in wood using a 45◦washer (Rothoblaas) [12]
This type of connection has a wide field of application, ranging from beams, to truss joints to
base hold downs to name a few. The assembly procedure, which depends on the application
situation, may start with fixing/holding the steel plate on to the timber member followed by
partial or full pre-drilling using an alignment guide. The geometric spacing, edge and end
distances have to be verified, and the screws should cross each other without intersecting in
the middle of the timber, which will be discussed later.
Krenn & Schickhofer [16], who were the first to make a scientific publication on the topic,
had the aim to investigate the group effect. Approximately 600 tension tests in full scale
where conducted at the Institute of Timber and Wood Technology at Graz University of
Technology. For estimating the design along-system-axis capacity for steel-to-wood con-
nections, they suggested to use a truss model, see Figure 3.25. The model showed good
32
==PAGE:45==
3.10 Joints with inclined screws and steel plates as outer members
correlation between observed and estimated values and could be the preferred design model
due to its practical simplicity.
The measured displacements at failure were reported to be in the range of 3-4mm indicating
that the dowel effect could be neglected. The friction contribution from the inclination may
be accounted for as it will develop as soon as the joint is loaded, which is especially the case
for thinner steel plates with low out-of-plane bending stiffness. The proposed friction coef-
ficient µ = 0.25 is aligned with the current standards, and the new proposal of the Eurocode,
even though values of up to µ = 0.32 were measured.
CL

3.10.0.1
Structural detailing
Krenn & Schickhofer suggested some general criteria for structural detailing of joints with
inclined screws. The most relevant are listed below, for further description see [16].
• screws shall be of same type, size and insertion angle
33
==PAGE:46==
3 Theoretical background
• screws shall be arranged so that no eccentricities to the system line occur
• The screws installation torque shall be between 60 % and 80% of the characteristic torsion
capacity of the screw e.g. using a torque wrench, or follow the technical approval.
• Fastener spacing and end distances shall satisfy technical approvals or standards
The ductility of inclined screws is generally limited by the deformation and yield behaviour
in tension. The ductility must therefore be assigned to another ductile member of the connec-
tion like the steel plates. The weak link analogy is used to determine which part of the joint
that should have lower capacity, in order to ensure the desired ductile failure. An example
to this is the local yielding of common angle brackets, where yield lines may form around
vertical anchor bolts. That being said, it should be kept in mind that the yield strength is
often higher than the assigned grade, as well as strain hardening increases the capacity in
tension. Both theses effects make it challenging to obtain the desired behaviour. To ob-
tain this desired ductile behaviour further detailed work concerning the yield behaviour of
the full system may be investigated experimentally or numerically, and parts of the material
used for the manufacturing should be tested upon arrival to ensure that its within the declared
performance.
The effective insertion length of the threaded part should be at least 4d ref. EN 1995-1-1
and ETA-11/0030. To avoid perpendicular tensile stress failure the screws can overlap 4d
in the system axis of the timber member (in a symmetrical joint), or some other means of
perpendicular reinforcement may be used. A benefit of crossing the screws is the additional
penetration length that can be acquired providing higher capacity, while the transverse rein-
forcement, e.g. screws, may be useful in situations where there are geometry constraints.
The effective insertion depth of the screws’ threaded part should be of a ratio that avoids
tensile failure in the screw, as this mechanism is brittle with little to no warning. Krenn &
Schickhofer reported that the use of screws with effective insertion depth of more than 16-
20d may result in head tear off / tensile failure. For high tensile strength screws this ratio
may vary.
The effective number of fasteners in a row along the grain, in common practice for timber
structures, is reduced by 10 - 15% depending on the intermediate spacing. Since joints with
inclined screws are not specifically covered by the current standards, it becomes unclear for
which nef to be used[16]. As the screws are primarily loaded axially it can be argued that
the section in EN1995-1-1 covering axially loaded screws shall be applied, which would
result in n0.9. Krenn & Schickhofer (2009) concluded that a reduction of a global factor
of 0.9n was sufficient as most of the tests showed little to no correlation with reduction in
strength for multiple fasteners in a row. The results are also in alignment with the technical
assessment of Rothoblaas’s ETA-11/0300, for which the effective number of fasteners in
ULS are determined by:
n =
{
nef = n
for n = 1
nef = max(n0.9 ; 0.9·n)
for n > 1
(3.42)
While it was suggested to reduce the estimated stiffness for a group of screws in serviceability

3.10.1
Withdrawal strength and capacity of axially loaded self-tapping screws
in the CLT element side face
Self tapping screws (STS) installed in wood have high load bearing potential in resistance
and stiffness when loaded in the screws axis. Partially or fully threaded screws installed at
an inclination to the grain e.g. 45◦are an efficient alternative to conventional laterally loaded
dowel type fasteners like nails, dowels and bolts [17]. Self tapping screws are frequently
used because of their simple and economic installation in solid and glue-laminated timber
products. Screws installed in the CLT narrow face are not subject of this work.
Joints with inclined screws are not particularly covered by the current standards. Therefore a
set of estimated withdrawal capacities will be investigated, enabling quantitative comparison
of the latest state-of-the-art work in the subject of matter, which also covers the new revision
of the EN 1995-1-1.
Withdrawal properties and/or capacities together with the influencing parameters have so far
been analyzed by Blaß et al. (2006), Uibel & Blass (2007), Blass & Uible (2007) Pirnbacher
et al. (2009) and Ringhofer et al. 2015. Their work consider the development of models for
predicting the axial withdrawal strength of screws in solid timber and CLT.
Although there are similarities in design provisions for solid timber (ST), CLT and Glulam
(GLT), the orthogonal build-up of CLT demands extra attention [2]. Unlike solid timber
(ST) and GLT, CLT’s production tolerances by current European standards allows for gaps
up to 4mm in the outer layers and up to 6mm in the core layers. The code EN 16351 -cross
laminated timber limits gaps up to 6mm. The influence of stress relieves and gaps have to
be taken into account especially for fasteners inserted in the CLT narrow face. To reduce the
probability of inserting fasteners in gaps in the side face it is suggested to penetrate at least
three layers (M=3, with M being the number of penetrated layers) according to Blaß & Uibel
2007 [2].
As the withdrawal strength is the most influential parameter for the axial capacity of threaded
screws, it becomes important to establish a solid theoretical foundation for the evaluation of
strength for optimal and safe design. Before comparing these estimates some definitions are
to be established.
3.10.1.1
Definitions
The interaction between the axially loaded fastener and the timber component is expressed
by the withdrawal strength fax [2]. In contrast to the withdrawal parameter f1 = fax ·π, the
withdrawal strength fax is defined as
fax = Fax,α,R
ℓef ·d ·π
(3.44)
35
==PAGE:48==
3 Theoretical background
where Fax,α,R is the fasteners capacity in the axial direction (equal to the withdrawal re-
sistance Fax,α,Rk according to EC5 [18]), d the nominal diameter, and ℓef as the effective
inserted thread length [2]. Subscript k refers to the characteristic value.
The angle between the screws axis and the grain direction is defined by α and the angle
between load and end grain of outer layers by β, as seen in Figure 3.26.
Figure 3.26 Definition of screw-axis to grain angle α and angle between load and grain direction of outer
layers β [2]
3.10.1.2
Withdrawal strength and/or withdrawal capacity
In the following, different methods for determining the withdrawal strength and/or the with-
drawal capacity, based on research or standards are investigated and compared. In some of
the literature the writers have determined to use the withdrawal parameter, which is why
some of the expressions may be manipulated to acquire the withdrawal strength, so that a
common basis for evaluation can be established. Although more than three parameters may
be used to develop a predictive model, the practical application becomes cumbersome. The
primary parameters for the withdrawal strength are; the diameter d, density ρ and effective
insertion length ℓe f .
1.
Eurocode 5 [18] is per now the legislative standard of design until a new revision is
commenced. The withdrawal strength is based on the work of Blass et al. (2006) having
performed 1212 withdrawal tests in solid wood [2]. Division by π is as mentioned to obtain
the withdrawal strength.

ter, thread pitch, density, insertion depth and withdrawal resistance of softwoods with aver-
age density of 430kg/m3. Some mention-able findings were that the insertion angle behaved
independent in the range α = {45◦- 90◦}, especially in a model only using three parameters,
and that the thread pitch did not have major influence on the withdrawal resistance. The
database was limited to angles equal to or greater than 45◦. Division by π is to obtain the
withdrawal strength.
fax,k = 0.0872

(3.47)
The equation only concerns insertion angles between [45◦; 90◦]. Equation (3.47) provides
significantly higher strength compared to Ec5 [18], up to 30% more. It is also stated that the
equations are valid for nominal diameters d ={4 ; 14}mm, for penetration depths between
20 and 140mm. The results are based on screws from different manufacturers, leading the
interpretation to be of a more general basis.
4.
Ringhofer, Brandner & Schichofer (2015) worked on developing a general set of equa-
tions that would cover solid wood, Glulam and CLT, especially due to the complex lay-up
of CLT. This implied defining a set of parameters, that depending on the angle, number of
lamellae and layer orientation, would provide a general approach, compared to the previous
investigations which were explicit to the material of investigation. This implies reduction in
strength caused by the possibility of insertion in gaps (0.5- 2mm) or intermediate zones of
layers, and the favourable effect of penetrating multiple layers N. Their work also aimed to
determine the axial stiffness of threaded screws as this had not been the focus of previous
work. The test data originated from 800 tests in the side face and 500 in the narrow face.

The parallel to grain/system line capacity, is derived based on the truss model, see Eq.(3.40),
and is quantitatively compared as shown in Figure 3.28. This comparison is primarily to
provide a spectra of capacity values for later reference when comparing with other means of
dowel type fasteners. The reason for the gap is the lack of suitable dimension of d = 13mm
from the manufacturer (Rothoblaas). Rothoblaas’s catalogue values are determined as the
horizontal projection of the withdrawal resistance in the direction of the system axis without
the friction contribution.
Figure 3.28 Comparison of parallel to system axis capacity for different methods for screws inserted at
45◦using the truss model
Figure 3.28 also shows that the steel capacity is limiting for a smaller diameter screw given
the same effective insertion length. The screws diameter to insertion length ratio becomes
a relevant factor when determining the size of screw for the design. With regards to safe
design the brittle failure of the screw failing in axial tension is undesirable. High tensile
strength screws are usually hardened may experience strain hardening and resilient strength,
this failure should be avoided.
The withdrawal strength and subsequently capacity varies within a large range. The values
for the given input parameters and a diameter of 11 millimetre range from fax.k = {3.13 ;
4.80} N/mm2. The trend is that the characteristic strength increases with the more recent
work together with a larger available data base compared to that of the current design pro-
40
==PAGE:53==
3.10 Joints with inclined screws and steel plates as outer members
vision. The most recent work of Ringhofer et al. which is also the design suggestion of the
new version of EN 1995-1-1 has significantly higher capacity, 34% for d = 11mm. This is
partly due to Ringhofer’s more general model better distinguishing the favorable strength in
the side face with more uniform density and homogenisation. The inclusion of ksys, kax.k and
kp in Equation 3.48 increases the strength significantly.
When comparing the methods and results from the withdrawal tests it can be seen that it is
reasonable to use a bilinear approach in practical design compared to the Hankinson criteria
in the current code (EC5).
For design purposes it is only permitted to use the characteristic strength and capacity ac-
cording to current standards or the manufacturers technical approval. In this case the latter
refers to Rothoblaas’s performance declaration in the ETA -11/0030 in which they have a
higher value for the withdrawal strength. In compliance with the current Eurocode the fric-
tion contribution in the truss model is limited to µ = 0.25, which is also the same for the
new Eurocode proposal. When looking at the parallel to force capacity in Figure 3.28, the
performance of the fastener based on the manufacturers technical assessment of the with-
drawal strength seems reasonable compared to that of the state-of-the-art work performed
by Ringhofer et al.[17]. Possibly even higher capacity may be acquired in the future.
3.10.2
Stiffness of joints with inclined screws and plates as outer members
Most of the previous work and research has been focusing on the withdrawal strength and
its modelling parameters rather then the stiffness. Ringhofer, Brandner and Schickhofer
(2015) [17] incorporated the axial stiffness of the fastener in their work. This together with
Rothoblaas’s ETA-11/0300 and the prEN are amongst the few sources on quantified work
on the topic. The method presented in ETA-11/0030 and prEN will be presented herein as
reference for later work.
According to the ETA-11/0300 the axial slip modulus for the threaded part of a screw for
the serviceability limit state should be taken independent of angle α to the grain as:
kser = 25·d ·ℓef
[N/mm] for fasteners in softwood
(3.58)
where d is the nominal diameter [mm] and ℓef is the effective anchoring length in the timber
member in [mm] according to EN1995-1-1:2008 ref. ETA-11/0030. Equation (3.58) only
considers the wood part and does not cover the friction interaction nor the stiffness in other
directions than the screws axis.
The equivalent spring stiffness in the direction of the system axis for the wood member, see
Figure 3.29, is determined by
kser,eq = kser ·cos2 α
(3.59)
where kser.eq is the equivalent parallel to force spring stiffness and α is the angle between the
screw axis and the system axis of the wood member. For the derivation of the expression see
Appendix B.2. The shortcomings of this prediction is the resistance provided by the friction
contribution between the steel plates and wood member, which would result in increased
stiffness.

For structural detailing it is important to distinguish between favourable and unfavourable
stiffness for the different structural performances of investigation. In the case of load dis-
tribution higher stiffness attracts forces and it becomes paramount that the stiffness’s are
not underestimated as this may lead to higher attraction of forces. On the other end of the
spectrum the real stiffness may be less, even half, of the theoretical, resulting in higher de-
formations than acceptable. It is therefore suggested to work with a range of values bounded
by an upper and lower extreme to cover the ends and intermediate values of the spectrum.
This may be organized as proposed in Eq. (3.63) which aims to determined the equivalent
stiffness for the two situations (ultimate) and serviceability limit state.

for SLS

3.11 Rigid diaphragm method
The engineer has to determine a suitable spectra for the given situation to ensure that the
outer extreemes are covered.
3.11
Rigid diaphragm method
The rigid diaphragm method assumes that the in-plane stiffness of the diaphragm has suf-
ficiently high stiffness so that its kinetic behaviour is similar to that of a rigid body. This
assumption is a good approximation for structures who in fact have stiff diaphragms like
cast-in-situ reinforced concrete. Mass timber structures have in general more slip from the
dowel type connections which affects the load progression. This reduced stiffness prohibits
the individual walls from attracting loads simply based on their relative area/size like the or-
dinary rigid diaphragm method assumes. A modification is therefore implemented for CLT
structures to better capture the real behaviour, see Wallner-Novak et al. (2014) [11], and
Pettersen [8].
If the floor diaphragm is sufficiently rigid compared to the walls, the lateral loads may be
distributed according to the walls relative stiffness. According Wallner-Nowak et al. (2014)
the comparative calculations with tie rods and shear brackets result in a relative stiffness of
B ∼ℓ1.5. The method described in the following is applicable to walls oriented perpendicular
to one another. For more complex problems FE formulation where the walls are modelled
as springs will provide a more accurate/reliable result.
Using a local coordinates system the determination of forces in the individual walls are
determined in the following. Unless the resultant lateral forces aligns with the shear centre
some additional torsional moment will arise from the eccentricity.
Bx,i = ℓ1.5 = |xE −xA|1.5
(3.64)
By,i = ℓ1.5 = |yE −yA|1.5
(3.65)

position of the plate centre given by

CHAPTER4
Basis for design
4.1
Design situation
The requirements for a structure’s safety is verified in the Ultimate Limit State (ULS), while
the comfort of the structure is verified in Serviceability State (SLS). It must be verified that
the applied stresses are at any time and any point lower than the material strength. In addition
to the cross sectional load-bearing capacity, stability failures like buckling, and joints must
be analyzed in ULS.
Load effects i.e. deformations, shear forces and more are determined using the STR / EQN
combination. The general equation is given in (4.1).
Ed = ∑
i≥1
KFIγG,iGk,i +KFIγQ,1Qk,1 + ∑
j>1
KFIγQ,jψ0,jQk,j
(4.1)
For details regarding (4.1) see EN 1990.
4.1.0.1
Safety classes
The structure of investigation is an office building with height of more than 12m to terrain
and therefore classifies as Consequence Class 3 (CC3). Due to the high risk of personal
injury the safety is increased using the factor KFI = 1.1 ref. DS/EN 1990-1 DK NA.
4.1.1
Partial coeﬀicient method
Determination of the level of safety is established using the partial coefficient method. The
applied load effect Ed must be less than or equal to the resistance Rd. The applied effect may
result in a force, stress or deformation whilst the resistance can be a capacity, stress limit or
deformation limit [8].
For wood structures including CLT the design resistance is verified in accordance with (4.2)
Ed ≤Rd
,
Ed ≤Rk · kmod
γM
(4.2)
Where Rk is the characteristic value of the capacity, kmod is the modification factor that ac-
counts for moisture content and load duration, and γM the partial coefficient for the material.
CLT uses the same kmod values as glued laminated timber.
4.1.2
Service class and load groups
The duration and moisture content affects the strength and deformation of wood based mate-
rials, which is considered applying the factor kmod. To account for the strength and stiffness,
structures are to be assigned to one of three service classes [18].
==PAGE:58==
4 Basis for design
• Service class 1 is characterised by a moisture content in the materials corresponding to a
temperature of 20◦C and the relative humidity of the surrounding air only exceeding 65% for
a few weeks per year e.g. indoor environment.
• Service class 2 is characterised by a moisture content in the materials corresponding to a
temperature of 20◦C and the relative humidity of the surrounding air only exceeding 85% for
a few weeks per year e.g. Covered ventilated structures
• Service class 3 is characterised by climatic conditions leading to higher moisture contents
than that in service class 2 e.g. unprotected outdoors structures sustainable to direct water.
CLT structures are not permitted to be used in service class 3 (CITE?)

CHAPTER5
Loads
The structure of investigation will be subjected to a combination of vertical and horizontal
loads which will be determined in the following.
5.1
Vertical loads
Vertical loads are all loads that have a force progression parallel to the direction of gravity.
This includes dead loads, imposed loads from people and nature loads from snow etc.
Waught Thisteleton Architects performed a thorough investigation of possible floor systems.
In common for these floor systems, are that all have additional mass in order to comply with
vibration and noise performance. The choice of floor structure is not critical for the determi-
nation of the critical shear walls as the most crucial load situation is in the construction phase
when the structure has no imposed dead load, beside the weight of the CLT elements them-
selves. Based on the recommendations and life cycle assessments the Knauf floor build-up
will be used in the static analysis. The floor structure and its build-up is presented in Fig-
ure 5.1, values are provided by Knauf.
Figure 5.1 Knauf floor build-up. Values provided by Knauf

specifications. The latter covers the factored values for ULS and SLS under different load
scenarios, see Table 5.2.

Example
For the purpose of working with quantified design data it is assumed that the bracket have

6.2
Horizontal load distribution
This section deals with the distribution of loads that are absorbed by the bracing system. For
the purpose of design, the loads are limited to effects from wind only. In addition, possible
eccentric wind effects are neglected. Other lateral effects such as second order effects, often
referred to p−∆, or seismic loads are not covered.
Load distribution in mass timber structures is in many ways similar to that for a cast in-situ
or prefabricated concrete structure. The main difference is that massive timber structures
have much less stiffness in their connections as the stiffness of the joints often are govern
by the embedment stiffness of the wood. This difference is paramount to understand when
distributing the forces as the key principle is that forces are attracted by stiffness and stiffness
is governed by the connections. This will become more apparent when investigating the load
distribution using FEM-Design.
Firstly, the surface loads have to be absorbed by the facade, which are then transferred to the
floor diaphragms, see Figure 6.5. For lower buildings with a large footprint the wind load
is constant over the height. For wind load, the horizontal loads accumulate storey-wise and
may be considered isolated for each storey [11].

53
==PAGE:66==
6 Load distribution
The wind load is assumed to come from two directions x−and y, at different time instances.
A selection of bracing walls are determined as shown in Figure 6.6, which are numbered
and labeled. If a statically admissible solution is found with a reduced number of bracing
walls, some flexibility is left for the architect and subsequently less manufacturing and work
is needed compared to anchoring all of the bracing walls. Selecting which walls to absorb
the lateral forces also aid the engineer in determining where the forces are to be taken. Re-
maining walls function to separate spaces and carry vertical loads. Only a few of the walls
along the x- direction are chosen as bracing walls as the facade is smaller leading to smaller
lateral forces. All the larger walls in y-direction are needed since the facade along x-direction
is significantly longer resulting in higher magnitude of forces.
It can be seen that the two walls 1y, and 5y have large cantilevers at either side. It can be
expected that these walls will be subject to a higher amount of forces than their neighbouring
walls, and therefore will be critical in terms of overturning and anchoring.
Bracing walls are required for at least three axes, preferably four or more. The bracing walls
must not have a common intersection, or all be parallel, both of which criteria are satisfied for
the structure seen in Figure 6.6. If these prerequisites are not met the structure is susceptible
to torsion or simply not capable of resisting the external effects.
6.2.1
Load distribution based on stiffness
As Previously mentioned the stiffness of the elements and their connections dictates where
the force progression takes place. If the floor diaphragm is rigid in its plane and the walls act
as soft spring supports the load will distributed to the neighbouring walls or springs. On the
other end of the spectra, if the diaphragm is soft and the walls are rigid the load will progress
to the nearest wall/support. In this project the orientation of the bracing walls results in the
shear centre aligning with the line of resultant wind force. Additional forces from torsion
therefore becomes negligible. As the diaphragm will translate nearly linearly the forces may
also be estimated using a simple beam model, with its limitation that the equivalent in-plane
stiffness have to be estimated in the correct range.
CLT walls have high in-plane stiffness compared to the connections to other plates or mate-
rial interfaces. The overall stiffness is primarily dominated by the brackets and joining meth-
ods. Different approaches e.g. analytical or numerical methods can be used to estimated the
inherent stiffness of the wall system, meaning the combined elastic resistance from the in-
dividual contributions as shear, bending, rocking and translation. Preliminary, the stiffness
of the wall system may be estimated based on performance requirements as any deviation
lower than required performance wouldn’t be acceptable. The level of in-plane deformation
usually vary between a low performance of l/300 to a higher performance of L/500. Using
the allowable deformation as a reference an approximate value can be obtained with some
experience.
(remove)? In the case of a 4m tall wall subjected to a lateral load of 50kN with a desired
performance of L/1000, equivalent to 4mm total deformation, gives an estimated equivalent
spring stiffness of 50kN/4mm = 12.5kN/m. This estimate in its simplification only applies
to walls of similar lengths, where the variation of the wall length effects the relative stiffness.
In other words, a longer wall will be subject to higher stiffness compared to a shorter wall due

Complex structures
For more complex structures the diaphragm will not necessarily be rigid or soft, but some
intermediate. The loads absorbed by the walls will therefore also be somewhere between the
rigid and soft behaviour as described introductory. The load progression process becomes
iterative since the choice of brackets influence the load progression. After the loads have
been distributed to the bracing walls and brackets have been determined, the updated stiffness
of these brackets are then introduced back into the model to verify that the previous results
have not altered, in the case that they have, new suitable brackets must be chosen.
6.2.2
Numerical investigation of load distribution
The FEA solver FEM-Design is used to perform a numerical investigation of the load pro-
gression from lateral effects. The rigid diaphragm method will function as basis for compar-
ison and validation of the numerical model. The floor plate and walls are modelled as com-
posite laminated shells with thickness of 240mm for the floor and 210mm for the walls. The
floor plates are supported vertically to avoid spatial bulging. Two exercises are performed
to analyse when the soft vs. rigid diaphragm behaviour occurs and what intermediate load
progression that can be expected.
55
==PAGE:68==
6 Load distribution
The floor panels are divided as shown in Figure 6.7 following the system lines. The joints
between the plates are then altered in stiffness. The model also considers the in-plane stiff-
ness of the CLT elements themselves, which will become apparent later effects the results.
The loads are simply verified in equilibrium where the reactions are the same as the applied
loads.
Figure 6.7 a) Panel division according to system lines b) FE model mesh
Exercise 1:
The first exercise is performed constraining the base of the walls. The in-plane stiffness of
the walls are the only contributor, compared to that of a fully rigid support. The floor plates
adjacent joints are than alternated in stiffness ranging in values gained from experience. A
lower value of k = 30000kN/m/m is representative of a connection with 8mm crossing
screws spaced at 200mm. Increasing the diameter to 10mm and reducing the spacing to
150mm, gives an estimated stiffness of k = 50000kN/m/m. The results of the first exercise
are presented in Figure 6.8.
Figure 6.8 Reaction forces from wind load for different joint stiffness’s of the floor plate.
The results of the first exercise show, as expected, that varying the joint stiffness effects the
magnitude of load each wall is absorbing. The last case sub-figure 6.8 d) shows that for
a ”rigid” plate the forces are more evenly distributed by the supports. This case is govern
by the plates in-plane stiffness, whereas using a fictitious diaphragm with infinite stiffness
would lead to the same result as for the rigid diaphragm method.
Exercise 2:
The wall to floor stiffness ratio is what dictates how the forces progress. Therefore in the

second exercise the floor plate is kept constant while the equivalent spring in the top of the
wall is altered. Figure 6.9 a) shows principally the location of the spring, which are equal
for all walls. In addition the the base is free to slide. The wall could have been removed
overall, and is merely there for graphical purposes. The stiffness range of the springs are
estimated based on performance requirements and numerical modelling of the wall. The
stiffness of the springs were found to be around 15 kN/m from a numerical model of the full

58
==PAGE:71==
6.2 Horizontal load distribution
6.2.4
Discussion
It can be said that the results provided by the FE model with fixed walls (6.11a) is on the
conservative side compared to the values given in Figure 6.11b, which uses unrealistically
high equivalent spring coefficients (ktop = 50MN/m). The results retrieved from the FE
model with an elastic diaphragm and elastic wall supports yield a favourable load situation
where the load is more evenly distributed by the parallel walls. Although the load scheme
is favourable there might be question to how good the relative stiffnesses are approximated.
If the model is wrong, the walls may absorb higher magnitudes of loads than they are de-
signed for leading to devastating outcomes. Rothoblaas have reported through testing of
their product that there is often large deviations in theoretical and actual stiffness. In general
the theoretical stiffnesses are far too high, in some cases up to double that of the experimen-
tal results [22]. This shows that the estimated dowel based stiffness in many cases can be
unpredictable, and it is therefore suggested to have some margin in the estimation of force
progression based on the stiffness properties to ensure that in the case of higher or lower stiff-
ness than expected, the structure would still perform as intended. Rothoblaas’s results show
that the actual stiffness were less than the theoretical, which implies that assuming higher
stiffness then the theoretical might be overly conservative and not particularly resource effi-
cient.

The highest axial compressive stresses arise from Load combination LC2, wind dominating
+ structural dead load unfavourable + imposed load. For the compressive stresses it can be
seen that the maximum stress is local to the bottom right corner. High local stress concen-
trations may lead to local yielding, which in itself is not crucial for the stability of the wall.
Buckling is most susceptible to occur in the middle height between the free vertical storey
height, which is why the stresses are retrieved from this area when performing buckling
verification. FEM-Design is not able to differentiate the location of the stress in its inter-
nal buckling check, which is why this is done manually. The software simply sums up the

axial and compressive contribution as prescribed in EN 1995-1-1 for each panel regardless
of the location, leading to unacceptable utilization’s. It may also be noted that in reality
the wall is connected to perpendicular butting shear walls that provides further resistance
to buckling from the connected interfaces. Buckling verification is therefore performed for
the load combination that gives the highest stress in the middle height, which was found to
be LC2 (wind dominationg + dead load unfavourable + imposed load) with a magnitude of
σc,buckl,d = 9.4MPa.

The higher utilisation in local compression is due to the wall’s corner being compressed
from the rocking behaviour. The axial stresses have further distributed once they reach the
middle height leading to a lower utilisation in buckling. The net shear value τnet,xy,d is high
due to the uncertainties of the actual stress distribution in the bottom part of the wall. As
the wall is capable of resisting a shear flow of more than 232kN/m shear failure should not
be a problem. To further back up this argument an exercise was performed. The detailed
calculations are presented in Appendix B.4. The buckling factor provided by Strusoft, here
kc = 0.78 is also verified manually using the method described in pro:Holz 2018 [7], see
Appendix B.6.
Discussion
It can be argued that having some local yielding of the corner is acceptable as this result in
stress redistribution, and is not critical for the stability of the wall. The axial buckling stresses
could have been extracted from the quarter high leading to a higher utilisation, compared
to the stresses from the middle height which are noticeably lower. The real buckling is
estimated to occur spatially as the free edge is partially supported along the height, resulting
in even higher capacity. The net shear value τnet,xy,d is high due to the uncertainties of the
actual stress distribution in the bottom part of the wall. As the wall is capable of resisting
a shear flow of more than 232kN/m shear failure should not be a problem. To further back
up this argument an exercise was performed for the same magnitude of force, with the base
firmly clamped which yielded a value of nxy = 210kN/m, similar to that of the cut sections
without the peaks (Figure 7.9).
7.2
Serviceability Limit State
The wall’s performance has to be verified for the characteristic or frequent load situation.
The wall system must suffice the inter-storey and global deflection criteria, usually in the
range of L/500 - L/400. Two methods have been performed to estimate the deflection of the
wall system, hand calculations following the theory presented in section 3.6, and FE-analysis.
In order to best capture the kinematic behaviour, the non-linear elastic uplift calculation is
performed for the FE-analysis. This simply implies that the supports that are predefined to
67
==PAGE:80==
7 Verification of wall system
only take compression will allow the elements to separate under tension. The input data in
terms of geometry and stiffness’s are the same as for the previous section, see Figure 7.2, with
the exception of the equivalent base hold down stiffness now being keq,t = 999MN/m/m.
Similar to the analytical in-plane shear stiffness the membrane part of the homogenized shell
stiffness is reduced by a factor of 0.75 to obtain the effective stiffness ref. [8]. The wind load
is applied with its characteristic value at each storey, Wk = {74.5, 74.5, 74.5, 74.5, 46.6 }kN,
where the smaller value relates to the roof elevation.
The resulting deflection of the wall-system from the FE-analysis, including structural and

and sliding is estimated to be an additional 5mm resulting in a final deformation at around
+/- 32mm. This is equivalent to a deflection criteria of L/625, which is higher than the sug-
gested global deflection criteria of L/500 ref. [7]. The high in-plane stiffness and negligible
slip from the stiff connection makes this a high performing system wall system. The detailed
calculations can be found in Appendix B.5.
