CHAPTER8
Base Hold Down connection
8.1
Design alternatives
The first question that arises when dealing with the lateral bracing system is how the forces
are to be transferred to the base and foundation. A selection of possible configurations are
shown in Figure 8.1. Considering all the factors explained below, option c stands out as the
most prominent alternative.
a)
b)
c)
d)
F
F
F
F
Figure 8.1 Selection of bracing solutions for wall systems, a) steel rod or cable connected to the top, b)
base hold down as suggested by MGA, Tall wood [1], c) conventional base hold down mounted
on the side face, d) concealed base hold down in the panel
When establishing which alternative to proceed with for detailed investigations, a compara-
tive analysis was performed. Some mention-able considerations are listed below
• low complexity
• simple manufacturing
• simple installation
• suitable tolerances
• economic design
• high performance i.e. stiffness / capacity
• preferably de-mountable for repair or inspection
Alternative (a) was suggested by the consortium as a high capacity and high stiffness solu-
tion. Inspired by concrete element design the rods or strands may be embedded inside or on
the exterior of the elements. This solution has potential, however there are concerns with
the detailing, load transfer at the top and bottom, and the overall complexity of the struc-
ture. The detailing and execution of the detailed design is much more complex and therefore
disadvantageous.
Alternative (b) is inspired by the Tall Wood report [1], where the base hold down (BHD) is
fastened to the sde face close to the narrow faces of the panel. This provides a more optimal
==PAGE:84==
8 Base Hold Down connection
internal moment arm as the BHD is connected at the panels outer edges. In the preliminary
analysis it became evident that the tensile strength of the wood was the weakest link. The
width needed to transfere the large forces lead to wider steel plates with a geometric centre
further away from the narrow side of the panel which is where the force is transfered to
the foundation. This additional eccentricity provides undesireable ”parasitic” forces in the
joint due to the eccentricity of the fastener group in relation to the line of force. Performing
detailed FE-analysis concerning the distribution of stresses over the width of the CLT panel
may show that the width can be reduced reducing the additioanl forces introduced in the
joint. Another disadvantage with this design is the clash between butting walls where the
connection is located.
Alternative (c) is a more conventional side face mounted base hold down. This design al-
lows for more flexibility regarding geometry and location of the connection itself. Although
it is preferable to have the connection at either end, it is possible to bring it towards the cen-
tre leaving room for butting walls connecting in the corners. Since the connection is face
mounted it will most likely require to be encapsulated regarding fire design. An advantage
with this type of design is the possibility to inspect and possible service/change it out if
needed as it is usually fastened with dowel type fasteners.
The last alternative (d) is similar to the previous (c) with the exception that the plate is
installed inside the wall element, similar to knife plates in Glulam. This solution can offer
higher capacity since the number of shear planes are increased and will also provide more
resistance under fire situation. The disadvantage is that it requires tight tolerances and more
manufacturing, especially considering the depth of the recess needed for the plate.
Out of the four, alternative (c) stood out as the one with the highest potential in terms of
fulfilling the list of requirements. The next question in hand is how the forces are to be
transferred between the material interfaces, wood-to-steel, and steel-to-concrete. The latter
can be achieved through conventional steel and concrete design, while the timber-to-steel
interaction is more challenging, mainly due to the geometry constrains and magnitude of
forces. A preliminary analysis for alternative fasteners were conducted which is shown in
Figure 8.2.

When looking at the stiffnesses and number of required fasteners it becomes evident that the
inclined screws are advantageous. The main difference in manufacturing is that the holes
need to be slotted rather than circular which is relatively simple for current manufacturing
methods. The main concern for inclined fasteners is the amount of ductility that can be
obtained in the joint which will be discussed later.
8.2
Design of connection
The anchor force has to be transferred from the CLT wall through all the connection com-
ponents to the foundation. It is therefore required that all the links have sufficient strength.
The list of failure mechanisms that has to be investigated and verified are listed below
(a) Tension failure of the wood
(b) Failure of gross steel plates
(c) Capacity of fasteners
(d) Geometry of fastener group
(e) Block shearing/ net tension failure
(f) Steel plates net
(g) Steel to concrete
Each of the mechanisms will be presented in the following. The design process is iterative,
mostly following the order of the list above. Because of this there might be deviations in the
order of which the findings are presented.
Stiffness of the connection
The stiffness of the full connection is estimated according to the theory presented in sub-
73
==PAGE:86==
8 Base Hold Down connection
section 3.10.2 (Stiffness of joints with inclined screws and plates as outer members). For
a connection with 4 × 11 screws, nominal diameter of 11mm, effective insertion length
ℓef f = 195mm with outer steel plates of 5mm × 700mm × 650mm the estimated stiffness
in ULS and SLS becomes:
kser.eq.ULS
= 1155MN/m
for ULS
kser.eq.SLS
= 999MN/m
for SLS
The stiffness per unit length for the finite element models are determined by division of the
width.
a) Tension failure of the wood
From the previous chapter chapter 7 force was determined to be TE.d = 1158kN. Assuming
an effective width of 700mm the axial tensile stress becomes σt.0.d = 11.03MPa.
A more detailed analysis was performed in Strusoft to investigate the effects of stress distri-
bution for a longer surface connection. The model has four levels of spring supports, each
with a spring coefficient of keq.l = 800MN/m/m over a length of 700mm. The eccentricity
of the connection is the same as presented in chapter 7. See Appendix B.7 for details in
determining the spring stiffnesses. The spring supports are deliberately high compared to
the full connection to ensure that the connection will attract a conservative amount of force
in the upper levels. The results of the FE analysis are shown as a colour stress palette in
Figure 8.3 together with the mesh. In order to avoid problems with discretization, three ex-
ercises where performed using higher order elements, eight node quadrilaterals. Figure 8.3
shows the results and mesh for the last analysis where the mesh is the finest.

Figure 8.3 Axial tensile stress in the wood surrounding the base hold down, (a) stress palette for σ′

The FE-model shows that the maximum tensile stresses are reduced compared to the simple
analytical estimate. With more knowledge on the stiffness of the connection the stresses may
be lowered further assuming that the distribution is more even for the different fasteners. The
axial tensile stress is extracted from the model as σx,0,d = 9.97MPa.
b) Failure of gross steel plates
The steel plates have each a thickness of 5mm and are of grade S355 with a yield strength
74
==PAGE:87==
8.2 Design of connection
of fyk = 355MPa and an ultimate strength fu = 510MPa according to EN 1993-1-1. The
steel plates are verified for gross tensile failure, net tensile failure and bearing resistance
according to EN 1993-1-1.
c) Capacity of fasteners
The parallel to grain capacity of inclined screws with steel plates as outer members were
determined in subsection 3.10.1. For a screw with nominal diameter of 11mm and effective
insertion length ℓef f = 195mm the characteristic capacity was found to be Fv.Rk = 23.94kN,
which including factors yields a design capacity of Fv.Rd = 19.5kN per screw. The yield
strength of the fasteners are fyk = 1000MPa.
The suggested fastener matrix consist of 4 rows of 11 screw pairs, for a total of 44 pairs
(n × m = 4 × 11). This includes the reduction nef due to the fasteners being installed in
parallel with the outer grain. The total capacity is estimated to be Fv.Rd.tot = 1545kN.
d) geometry of fastener group
The spacing of fasteners follows the recommended minimum spacing requirements compli-
ant with Rothoblass’s ETA-11/0030, which are the same values as presented by Blaß & Uibel
[19], [23]. The outer grain is used as reference for the grain direction. The required spacing
and end distances together with the adopted values for a screw with a nominal diameter of
11mm are shown in Table 8.4.

e) Block shearing/ net tension failure
The capacity of the connection has to be verified for block shearing / group tear-out. Based on
the geometry of the connection with screws crossing from both sides, block failure is the only
relevant failure mode. Group tear out is relevant for fasteners inserted partially in the material
side like nails and plate screws. Figure 8.6 shows five possible failure modes that have to be
investigated. CLT elements have additional transverse reinforcement from the orthogonal
75
==PAGE:88==
8 Base Hold Down connection
layers, which contribute to prevent block shearing by taking shear stresses perpendicular
to the grain in the crossing layers. The crossing layers ability to take axial stresses in the
transverse direction minimizing the possibility of splitting reducing the number of failure
modes to be checked for CLT to case (d) and (e).
Figure 8.6 Failure modes in a connection. Image from Cabrero and Yurrita (2018)
The applied method is provided in Annex A in EN 1995-1-1. The method states that the
block failure is governed by the max resistance provided by the net tensile resistance (red
dashed line) or the net shear resistance (blue dashed lines) in Figure 8.7. For CLT, the shear
failure planes should be investigated for both ”gross” shear and net shear whichever yields
the higher capacity.

Ductility
The ductility of the connection is of concern due to the connections high stiffness and the
limited ductility of inclined screws loaded in tension. The ductility must therefore be ob-
tained by some other member in the connection which has the ability to undergo relatively
large deformations without loosing its strength., e.g. the steel plates. Using a thinner outer
steel plate (5mm) allows the steel to be ”weak” enough to deform in tension as well as around
fasteners from bearing stresses. It should be kept in mind that the yield strength of the de-
livered material is often higher than the declared grade which makes it more challenging to
design the intended weaker link. It is also important to ensure that the screws have a suffi-
cient diameter to effective insertion length ratio so tensile failure of the screw will not occur
(ℓef f ≤16d −→20d). For this reason the design uses screws of at least d = 11mm (17.7d)
which should provide sufficient strength so that the screw will fail in withdrawal before the
head tears off.
8.2.2
Discussion
There is always room for improving, or alternate the design. If the connection is to be in-
stalled at site it may be reasonable to increase the height to the first row to allow better access
for tooling. The clearance presented has been calculated with a relatively tight tolerance. If
the bracket is mounted before shipping this issue will be resolved. Design alternative 3 Fig-
80
==PAGE:93==
8.2 Design of connection
ure 8.11 shows a possible solution to mounting the bracket to a knee wall. This is favourable
in cases where the height of the wall is different on both sides as they sometimes are around
an elevator shaft. This design in contrast to the two others have no possibility to obtain yield
lines around the connection making it questionable in terms of ductility. The only ductility
obtained comes from the bearing deformation around the fasteners and bolts. It may there-
fore be better to use an extended bracket reaching all the way to the foundation past the knee
wall similar to alternative one and two.
It is favourable that the base hold down connection can be inspected and possibly changed
if needed. For that reason all the designs use plates mounted on the exterior that can be
removed. It would also be possible to connect the two metal plates with a bearing plate
underneath, however, that makes the connection permanent and interchanging it nearly im-
possible.
Sufficient tolerances are important for connections in general. CLT elements usually work
with tolerances in millimetres while concrete work can be in centimeters. For alternative
1 and 2, it is suggested to use a mounting plate at the surface or embedded in the concrete
to ensure that the threaded steel rods have the designated spacing. in the lateral direction
there is tolerances for the bolt holes around the threaded rods, normally about 2 millimetres.
The tolerances in the vertical direction may be accompanied through the use of steel shims.
alternative 1, further tries to solve this problem by elevating the wall system when installed
for later to be under-packed using a dryer mix of concrete. This allows the adjustment in
the vertical direction for the installation, while the final strength is achieved later once the
under packed concrete has hardened. The third alternative may achieve a tight fit by use of
shims packed at the sides of the knee wall. In general it is required to have tighter tolerances
when placing the threaded rods in the foundation. The steel plates can be fastened once the
elements are lifted in place
8.2.3
Design optimisation
The design presented is relatively wide compared to its height when comparing with more
traditional base hold downs. The width of the connection will to some extent lead to uneven
stress distribution in the connection in the elastic spectra from the linear stress distribution.
To reduce this effect and take advantage of the increased moment arm it can be beneficial
to increase the height of the connection. This also implies changing the fastener matrix
having more screw pairs in parallel with the outer grain. The initial design was limited by
the tensile capacity of the wood which is why the necessary width of approximately 700mm
was implemented. In an effort to see how the stresses vary with the change of geometry a
FE analysis for a altered design was conducted. The alteration has a fastener matrix of 6×8
pairs of screws leading to a total similar to before. The width is reduced to 550mm. The
stiffness per unit length for each row of fasteners were estimated to be keq.l = 555MN/m/m.
The results and discrete mesh are shown in Figure 8.12.
81
==PAGE:94==

(a)  
(b)  
Figure 8.12 Axial tensile stress in the wood surrounding the base hold down, (a) stress palette for σ′

finite element mesh around the spring supports (eight node quadrilaterals)
It can be seen in Figure 8.12 that the reduced stiffness per row leads to a similar tensile
stress level for the wood member. Further optimisation is subject to future work and is not
investigated in detail here.

==PAGE:95==
CHAPTER9
Vertical panel interface
This chapter concerns the investigation and comparison of different joining techniques used
for connecting CLT panels.
The vertical joint between the panels is required to have sufficient capacity and stiffness.
The stiffness of the joint plays a role in the kinematic behaviour of the wall i.e. coupling
behaviour. To achieve the desired kinematic behaviour, and thus maintain the internal mo-
ment arm for the base hold down, the wall must achieve single panel behaviour or close to
single panel behaviour. This simply means that the panel must move as one unit and not
rotate about the toe individually as this leads to higher anchor forces. Different designs will
be investigated to see whether they are capable of transferring the forces within reasonable
design execution. Factors such as manufacturing and assembly will also be evaluated as they
play an important role to whether the design is practical to implement.
The connection has to be able to transfer the shear forces between the panel segments which
involves the verification of the following list.
• capacity of fasteners
• geometry of fasteners (spacing)
• fastener stiffness - coupling behaviour
• shear capacity of net wood - if section is reduced
All of these criteria have to be satisfied in order for the connection to be applicable.
9.1
Joint technique alternatives
A selection of vertical jointing techniques that will be investigated are shown in Figure 9.1
and Figure 9.2
a)
b)
c)
d)
Figure 9.1 Section view of possible vertical joining techniques, a) internal metal plate, b) half-lap joint,
c) crossing inclined screws d) external lap plates
==PAGE:96==
9 Vertical panel interface
Alternative (a), internal metal plate, consist of a recessed groove in which an aluminium
alloy plate is inserted. Half the joint can be installed at the manufacturing site to reduce on
site installation. The fasteners consist of are self boring dowels (SBD), that when paired with
an aluminium alloy provides a much simpler installation compared to steel. Currently, only
self boring dowels of 7.5mm can be applied which limits the capacity of the joint. It would
be possible to increase the capacity having two plates, however, this makes the installation
and tolerances more challenging.
Alternative (b), half-lap joint, is achieved through routing out a recess of half the thickness
of the CLT plate. The joint is screwed together using self tapping screws (STS) at site. A
disadvantage with this joint is the reduced section that has to carry the shear force, making
it a possible weak link. The joints advantage is its simple form in terms of installing screws
at site.
Alternative (c), crossing inclined screws, is most typical for floor plates as the joint can
transfer forces in all three main directions. Orienting the joint vertically makes it more
challenging for the workers to install the screws as gravity is working unfavourably. The
joint can achieve high stiffness as both fasteners are active. It is also possible to have the
screws carry axially by spatially inclining the screws in two directions. Meaning that the
screws are rotated 45◦in the direction of the force. This however, further complicates the
geometry and is therefore not suggested.
Alternative (d), crossing inclined screws, is achieved by routing a recess of the same thick-
ness as the lap plates. The lap plates can consist of plywood, LVL or a similar higher strength
wood based product. The lap plate material is also required to have sufficient in-plane
strength. The joint achieves higher capacity by having screws at both sides of the wall.
The disadvantage of this joint is the reduced stiffness due to the force having to pass through
two sets of shear planes.
As an alternative to carry shear forces between the members by dowel type connections, shear
keys may be applied. Shear keys generally provide high stiffness and resistance in shearing.
There are predominantly two types of shear keys for mass timber wall systems, Wooden
shear keys integrated in the panels or metal shear keys installed at site, see Figure 9.2.
Direct bearing
10 -15mm gap
e)
f)
Side view
g)
Figure 9.2 Shear keys, (e) direct bearing of the wood (f) joint to be filled with a hardening compound (g)
SLOT connector by Rothoblaas [22]
Alternative (e) and (f), wooden shear keys, are achieved by cutting and routing out the ge-
84
==PAGE:97==
9.1 Joint technique alternatives
ometry in a computer numerical controlled machine (CNC). The wooden shear keys can be
performed with tight tolerances or a gap for later to be filled with a compound that hardens.
In both cases the shear keys carry the load through contact stresses in the interfaces which
have to be carried by shear stresses from the contact point to the full thickness of the wall.
Alternative (g), metal shear key, is a commercially available shear connector manufactured
by Rothoblaas. A slot is routed in the panel where the shear key is to be placed once the wall
plates are lifted in place. The connector is wedge shaped to aid the installation and alignment
of the wall segments. Additional screws are installed to clamp the walls tight.
Based on a preliminary analysis the wooden shear keys showed to have insufficient shear ca-
pacity in the net section between the contact point and full wall. In this preliminary analysis
a relatively high shear flow was used nxy = 150kN/m, as there is uncertainty to what level of
vertical stiffness that can be achieved. More detailed investigation may reveal that the joint
is suitable, however, based on the preliminary analysis the joint will not be quantitatively
compared.
Shear flow
Before attending to estimate the amount of fasteners needed in the joint, the magnitude of
the shear flow has to be determined. The magnitude of the shear flow depends on the kine-
matic behaviour, the stiffness of the vertical joint, boundary conditions and more. A bench-
marking example will be presented in the following to show the concept and aid to verify
the outcome of the numerical investigation to come later.
Three cases are presented in Figure 9.3 below. The fully rigid case (a) having the highest
stiffness will take the ordinary parabolic shape. The fully separated case (c) will have less
resistance to deformation and take the shape of two independent parabolas. The intermediate
case (b) having a joint stiffness will land somewhere between the two first extremes. What
is important to note is the magnitude of shear flow the intermediate case can attract. Since
the shear flow is a function of the stiffness of the joint, a less stiff joint will also attract less
shear forces.
85
==PAGE:98==
9 Vertical panel interface
(a)
(b)
(c)
௫௬
Figure 9.3 Principal shear flow nxy and deformation for varying vertical joint stiffness. Deformation originates
form characteristic wind. (a) fully fixed, (b) joint with kv = 50MN/m/m, (c) fully free joint
A high stiffness will resemble full composite action, while low stiffness takes the form of
individual panel behaviour.
Numerical analysis of the joint shear flow
The process of determining the vertical joint shear flow is iterative. The amount of shear
flow attracted to the joint is a function of the joints stiffness, with higher stiffness resulting
in higher magnitude of shear flow. The investigation is therefore performed in steps. To
begin with, a relatively high vertical joint stiffness is assumed. The high stiffness will attract
higher magnitude of forces that are then used to determine the required number of fasten-
ers needed. Once the number of the fasteners are determined, the associated stiffness of the
fasteners can be estimated. A new analysis is then performed for the new and more represen-
tative stiffness. If a high joint stiffness is initially assumed, the second analysis with lower
stiffness will lead to less shear flow, which in turn allows for reduction of fasteners. Usually,
a reasonable utilisation of the connection can be found within two or three iterations. The
aim is to determine a statically admissible system that has a little but not too much move-
ment. Allowing the wall segments to slip in the vertical joint has the benefit of reducing the
connectors making the design more economic and feasible in practice. A completely rigid
joint may result in too many fasteners or fasteners spaced too close.
The vertical joint is analysed for three different vertical joint stiffnesses of kv = 30MN/m/m,
kv = 50MN/m/m and kv = 80MN/m/m, see Figure 9.4(a), (b) and (c) respectively. Based
on experience these stiffnesses represent the spectrum of achievable joint stiffness for dowel
type fasteners or similar.
86
==PAGE:99==
9.1 Joint technique alternatives
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 9.4 Connection Shear flow nxy for two different vertical joints stiffnesses (a) joint with kv = 30MN/m/m,
(b) joint with kv = 50MN/m/m, (c) joint with kv = 80MN/m/m
If the wall was analysed as a fixed cantilever beam the analytical result would yield a parabolic
max shear flow of nxy.analytical = 142.2kN/m. It is seen that the lower stiffness results in
lower shear forces in the joint. However, it is important that the joint has sufficient stiffness
so that single wall behaviour is obtained.
9.1.1
Numerical investigation of coupling behaviour
The kinematic behaviour of the wall impacts how the forces are absorbed by the hold down.
To ensure that the kinematic behaviour of the wall tends towards single wall behaviour, a
numerical analysis in FEM-Design was conducted. The two possible rotational behaviours
of the segmented wall depend on multiple parameters. At equal conditions the rotational
behaviour is determined by the stiffness relation between the vertical joint and the base hold
down, (kv/keq.t). For higher total stiffness of the vertical joint the wall tends towards single
wall behaviour.
In order to establish the required level of vertical stiffness per unit length of the wall a series
of models with varying vertical joint stiffness were analysed. The stiffness of the base hold
down was kept constant throughout, which had a magnitude of keq.t = 1155MN/m/m over a
length of 700mm, similar to the ULS stiffness. It is conservative to use the upper limit of the
stiffness, as a higher keq.t of the base hold down will restrain the uplift, leading to coupling.
The results from the FE analysis are presented inn Figure 9.5.
87
==PAGE:100==
9 Vertical panel interface
(a) kv = 10 MN/m/m
(b) kv = 15 MN/m/m
(c) kv = 20 MN/m/m
(d) kv = 25 MN/m/m
(e) kv = 30 MN/m/m
(f) kv = 40 MN/m/m
Figure 9.5 Reaction forces from varying vertical joint stiffnesses
The results show the predicted coupling behaviour, where lower vertical joint stiffness results
in coupling that leads to a higher tension force in the anchor. When the vertical joint stiffness
per length reaches a magnitude of 30 - 40 MN/m/m the coupling behaviour becomes less
pronounced indicated by the blue compression zone at the lower right corner of the left wall
segment. The anchor force regains the ULS magnitude of approximately Td = 1158kN for
case (f).
The stiffness estimate of the vertical joint and the base hold down may be far off from the
real stiffness. As long as these errors are proportional, the result will be similar.
9.2
Comparison of joint techniques, capacity and stiffness
Now that the vertical shear flow is established the different connections can be quantitatively
compared. Each connection type is calculated to be able to withstand the shear flow for
the connection’s estimated stiffness. This means that for a stiffness of less than 50kN/m/m
the shear flow corresponding to Figure 9.4(b) is applied (nxy = 86kN/m) and similar for a
stiffness up to 80kN/m/m, (nxy = 97kN/m). Further iterations have not been conducted for
the comparison. The results are summarised in Figure 9.6 showing the stiffness and spacing
of the joining techniques. The values presented are relative to the amount of force that is
able to propagate in the joint and should therefore not be directly compared.

Figure 9.6 Reaction forces from varying vertical joint stiffnesses
It can be seen that both the spacing, analogous to the capacity, and the stiffness vary between
the joining techniques. Joints that require many tightly spaced fasteners are less applicable
in practice as they are time consuming to install e.g the half lap joint. In general all the
dowel type joints have relatively tight spacing as they are all below 150mm. The inclined
screw joint has the added benefit that both fasteners contribute to the capacity increasing the
spacing between the screw pairs. The inclined screw technique is usually applied to floor
slabs as the installation does not work against gravity. For this reason the technique may
not be favourable from the perspective of installation. When comparing the stiffnesses, it
can be seen that the lap plate method falls short in terms of coupling behaviour. The joint is
required to have a magnitude of more than 30MN/m/m to ensure single panel behaviour.
The remaining joining techniques all have stiffnesses higher than this criteria and are suitable
in terms of coupling behaviour.
Three out of the five methods are more suitable in this instance, Internal lap plate, inclined
screws or SLOT connector. The slot connector could be the most favourable due to its short
installation time at site and reasonable spacing, especially if the connector is installed at
both sides of the wall, (900mm). All of the connections will be able to provide the needed
performance and it is therefore up to the project to determine which solution they think is
the best. The variation in stiffness has a negligible effect for the deflection in serviceability
limit state. The details of the calculations can be found in Appendix B.8.
Discussion
In terms of coupling behaviour it is beneficial to have some slip in the joint to reduce the
amount of attracted forces. The accuracy of the estimated stiffness is always in question.
As it was shown in the coupling analysis a lower joint stiffness will lead to higher anchor
forces. If the actual stiffness is much less than the theoretical, the coupling behaviour may
take place. It may be an idea to have some margin in the limit of the stiffness to ensure the
desired kinematic behaviour.
When comparing the installation time of the different joining techniques the dowel type
connections with tight spacing falls short in terms of efficiency. It should be noted that the
required amount of needed fasteners relates to a point in time where the building envelope
is completed and the structure is subject to full wind load. Until then, the forces that can
develop in the joint are noticeably smaller. This means that not all of the fasteners are needed
89
==PAGE:102==
9 Vertical panel interface
under the time of installation allowing the workers to install the remaining screws at some
later point. The installation of the remaining fasteners can then be performed when there
is little work on site. In that way the joints with many fasteners require less time for the
preliminary installation and can thus be more competitive.
90
==PAGE:103==
CHAPTER10
Experimental testing of
Inclined screws with steel
plates as outer members
10.1
Introduction
Screws installed in wood have high load bearing potential in resistance and stiffness when
loaded in the screws axis. Partially or fully threaded screws installed at an inclination to the
grain e.g. 45◦are an efficient alternative to conventional laterally loaded dowel type fasteners
like nails, dowels and bolts [17]. Self tapping screws are frequently used because of their
simple and economic installation in solid and glue-laminated timber products.
The application of inclined screws in joints with steel plates as outer members were first
presented by Krenn & Schickhofer (2009)[16]. Their work concerned the capacity of joints
with inclined screws and thick outer steel plates with specialized hole geometry in glued
laminated and solid timber. They tested joints with screws having an inclination of 30◦and
45◦for a single and multiple pairs of screws. They found that there was no noticeable group
effect. Krenn & Schickhofer also proposed a simple calculation model, see section 3.10, to
estimate the capacity of the individual screw based on the withdrawal resistance and friction
between the wood and steel.

==PAGE:104==
10 Experimental testing of Inclined screws with steel plates as outer members
Rothoblaas has developed a 45◦washer to supplement the installation of inclined screws
that removes the need of thick steel plates with specialized hole geometry, see Figure 10.1.
The 45◦washer is seated in a slot allowing the use of thinner steel plates opening up to
further possibilities and allowing the connections to be more economical in terms of material
consumption and manufacturing.
As it was seen in chapter 8 the stiffness of the connection plays an important role on the
stress distribution and deformation. The stiffness of interest is the parallel to force stiffness,
as this parameter is so far estimated by calculation models. The design that is being tested
is a scaled down version, only one pair of fasteners, of the proposed base hold down design
in chapter 8 (Base hold down connection).
To compliment the testing many of the tests were filmed. A short video highlighting some
of the failure mechanisms can be found by following the link here.
10.1.1
Objective and aim
The aim of the experimental testing is to determine the capacity and stiffness of the fasteners
in the design for the base hold down, see chapter 8, and to validate the design model proposed
by Krenn & Schickhofer. This also includes the determination of friction contribution due
to the inclination of the screws axis. The friction contribution is determined by performing
two test series under equal conditions, where one of the test series has Teflon tape between
steel and wood.
In order to have comparative results regardless of analytical models, a series of tests with
screws positioned perpendicular to the grain were also performed to compare the stiffness
and capacity of the two joining techniques. As it was not possible to use the same diameter
screw for the perpendicular tests, screws with a nominal diameter of 10mm were used.
10.2
Material and methods
10.2.1
Timber
Tests were performed in Cross Laminated Timber (CLT) declared as strength class C24 for
all layers and Laminated Veneer Lumber (LVL). The density of the CLT revealed that the
strength class, in terms of dowel type fasteners, should be corrected to C30 as the average
gross density of the CLT specimen was found to be ρmean = 468kg/m3. The CLT specimens
were of high quality with little to no gaps or cracks. The CLT specimens were built up from
European spruce (Picea Abies) most likely from Austria, while the LVL originated from
Finnish Spruce.
The CLT specimens were stored in a climate chamber at 20◦/ 65% RH from delivery until
testing, while the LVL specimens were stored indoors in room temperature, but not in a
climate chamber as the moisture content at delivery were closer to the desired level of 12%.
For the CLT samples, measurements of the moisture content were taken after testing at an
insertion depth of around 80mm. The Moisture Content (MC) was measured to be around
11-12% for the CLT specimens, and 10-12% for the LVL.
92
==PAGE:105==
10.2 Material and methods
Two sizes of specimens were delivered, CLT with dimension (length × width × thickness)
900mm×600mm×220mm consisting of a 7-2 build-up meaning that the two outer layers
run in parallel, and LVL with dimensions 900mm × 600mm × 161mm. The dimensions
were initially ordered based on a larger partial full scale setup, which had to be further
scaled down due to limited access to test machines with high enough capacity. As a re-
sult, the specimen were cut in half in the length direction yielding a length and width equal
to 450mm×600mm. The width allowed for three tests to be performed in the same speci-
men with a distance between the tested areas of 100mm. All tests had a distance from the
screws insertion point to the loaded edge of 150mm to ensure sufficient boundary conditions
and minimum spacing. The collected data of the CLT and LVL specimens is tabulated in
Table 10.2 and Table 10.3.

10.2.2
Fasteners
Three types of screws were used for the tests, two of which were self tapping fully threaded
(STFS) of product type VGS11x225 and VGS11x175 for CLT and LVL respectively, and
the third, a plate connector screw, under the product name HBS-plate. All the screws were
delivered by Rothoblaas, and had a declared yield strength of 1000MPa. The fully threaded
screws are accompanied by a 45◦washer referred to by VGU, which is designed to work with
the VGS conical head screws. The technical specifications of the fasteners can be found
in the technical assesment ETA-11/0030. Tensile tests of the screws were not conducted,
mainly due to the design of the test, were the failure was intentionally assigned to the timber
part.
Figure 10.5 Screw of type VGS 11x175mm together with the 45◦VGU washer
10.2.3
Teflon tape
Teflon tape was used to reduce the friction between the steel plates and wood specimens.
The Teflon tape (SCAN-TEF 02-12SPX) had a thickness of 0.12mm, and was applied to
both parts, steel and wood, in the full contact area. The tape on the steel plates were re-used
up to three test, due to the negligible damage for each test.
Friction interaction between Teflon - Teflon was control measured using a simple pull block
configuration, see Figure 10.6 (c). Two steel blocks, 2.91kg and 7.91kg with Teflon tape
underneath, were pulled across a layer of Teflon tape attached to an LVL specimen using a
force gauge, see Figure 10.6(d).
34 friction tests were conducted for the 2.91kg steel block, which yielded a static friction
coefficient of µs = 0.203 with a standard deviation of sy = 0.014 and CoV = 6.8%. The
kinetic friction coefficient was found to be close at µk = 0.188 with standard deviation sy =
94
==PAGE:107==
10.2 Material and methods
0.012 andCoV = 6.5%. The same was found for the 7.91kg steel block, a friction coefficient
of µs = 0.192 with sy = 0.01 and CoV = 4.7%. Although the Teflon tape did not yield
a desirably low friction coefficient, it did however provide a smooth, even and predictable
material interface with a similar static and kinetic friction coefficient which is important for
the validation of the calculation model.
(a)
(b)
(c)
(d)
Figure 10.6 Teflon tape, (a) Teflon tape on CLT block, (b) Teflon tape on steel plates, (d) Measure of
friction coefficient using a steel block and force gauge
General comments
In two of the C1 tests the machine was manually stopped around its limit at 100kN. In
some cases of the C1 test series the screw in torque exceeded the 30kNm value provided by
Rothoblaas. Test C1.4 revealed that one screw had intercepted the other, destroying 40-50
mm of the threads leading to premature failure. This test result was therefore excluded from
the result data.
The outer steel plates started to show signs of spatial bulging around the 45◦washer as ex-
pected. The steel plates were re-used for several test with this additional permanent defor-
mation. The argument for reusing the plates, besides the economical, is that the area beneath
the screw that bulges away from the wood, regardless of the initial steel plate state, would
not contribute for the friction part of the resistance. However, a new set of plates were used
for the friction test in an effort to start the tests on equal basis.
The screws were pre-drilled with a 6mm diameter drill bit to a depth of 30mm, and torqued
to 30kNM. Pre-drilling only 30mm was intentional to both simulate real conditions at site,
where pre-drilling to full depth is more time consuming, and the fact that Ringhofer et al.
(2015) [17] reported that pre-drilling had little impact on the withdrawal strength.
After working with the installation of the joints the experience was similar to as expected.
The screws were relatively easy to install, and only required simple hand tools. A drill with
an additional handle due to the high torque would be recommended.
Extraction of the screws after failure was easy in all cases since the screws only had minor
bending deformation. This was also the case for the LVL tests where the reduced diameter
to length ratio resulted in even less noticeable bending deformation of the screws. Screws
were extracted from all tests, and head tear off did not occur in any test series.
It may be advised to increase the plate thickness from 5mm to 6mm for high load scenarios
to avoid unreasonable large permanent deformation around the screws washers. Depending
95
==PAGE:108==
10 Experimental testing of Inclined screws with steel plates as outer members
on the installation scenario, it may also be suggested to use a set screw to fix the steel plate
in place while the 45◦screws are installed. The screws, due to their orientation, strive to
displace the plate in the direction of the screw axis under installation, as could be expected.
Using a set screws is also recommended for future testing to ensure that the steel plates are
leveled when the load is initiated, avoiding potential premature failure due to uneven loading
in the test set-up.
10.3
Test set-up
The tests were carried out in an Instron 5982 Universal Test Machine with a 100kN load cell.
The speed of the tests were determined as described in EN 26891 clause 8.2. Estimation of
the failure load Fest for each test series was based on the calculated mean capacity followed
by an initial test run. In cases where the deviation between the estimated capacity and actual
capacity was high, the following tests in the series would be corrected. Characteristic values
are calculated according to DS/EN 14358:2016, where the strength parameters are assumed
to follow a normal distribution.
The ultimate capacity for inclined screws was estimated using the truss model by Krenn &
Schikhofer with the withdrawal strength estimated by the model presented by Ringhofer, et
al. (2015). The Ringhofer model provides a higher resistance due to the additional parame-
ters included in the model that accounts for the insertion face, gaps and number of penetrated
A
A

C
D

Figure 10.7 Test set-up showing specimen restrained by the reaction frame and the LVDT reading of the
reference hardwood block. The second LVDT, seen behind the load transfer block was not
used
96
==PAGE:109==
10.3 Test set-up
layers. In order to find a close approximation to the expected ultimate resistance, the mean
values for the densities that were measured, was used in the calculation model. The fric-
tion coefficient was also assumed to be higher than µ = 0.25 and were preliminarily set to
µ = 0.30.
The CLT test series C1 deviated from the loading protocol stated in EN 26891:1993 as there
was more than 25% variation between the estimated max load and the actual Fmax. It was
not possible to set a higher max load than 75kN in the test machines software due to its
limitation at 100kN. The load vs. time graph, see Figure 10.10, shows that the machine
increases the load until failure at a premature stage compared to what EN 26891 states.
For the test series with inclined screws the Linear Variable Differential Transducer (LVDT)
was fixed to the wood specimen, with the tip reading of the vertical relative displacement of
the screws head. This placement enabled the direct reading of the relative along grain vs.
screw displacement, without the contribution ofthe steel plates bearing stiffness. The LVDT
was initially placed directly in the screw head, however, video recordings revealed that this
placement was unreliable and that the LVDT had moved during testing, resulting in invalid
data. A reference block was therefore hot-glued on to the screws head, see Figure 10.8 (e)
and Figure 10.8(f). For this reason the stiffness of the first three C1 tests are disregarded in
the statistical analysis.
To ensure that each pair of steel plates were installed at the right elevation and distance to
the unloaded edge a simple contraption was constructed to aid the installation accuracy and
speed, see Figure 10.8(c). The contraption ensured that the centre line of the force would
be 200mm from the unloaded side in addition to support the steel plate vertically while the
screw was driven in.
For the test series with perpendicular screws an additional pair of steel plates were manufac-
tured with dimensions (490mm×75mm×4mm). The variation in geometry is assumed to
not lead to noticeably different results as the magnitude of the failure load is estimated to be
around 25kN−30kN. The spacing between screws in the direction of the grain is the same
at 140mm, whereas the edge spacing is 150mm, equal to the inclined tests. The holes are
centrally located as the screws will not intersect in the middle like the inclined screws.
Additional pictures from the experimental testing can be found in Appendix C.2

Deviating from EN 26891:1993 the stiffness of the connection was additionally determined
by linear regression for two linear ranges k1 and k2, see Figure 10.10. Alternatively, the linear
ranges may be determined according to the method used by Ringhofer et al. (2015) where
the linear range is determined as the set of points where the force increment Fi+1 −Fi vs.
time is constant. The way the Instron test machine loads the specimen, through a feedback
loop referencing the change in movement, leads to a relatively jagged curve that is more
challenging to post analyse, which is why this method was not implemented.

10.4
Test results
The results from the test program are presented in the following as force vs. displacement
diagrams and tabulated values for each series. In general, it can be said that all the tests
showed higher than expected capacities. The failure was observed as withdrawal failure in all
cases and the strength decreased slowly with increased displacement until the predetermined
limit of 15mm was reached.
Test series C1
For test series C1, it was observed that the load transfer block remained horizontal until
failure. It is therefore assumed that the load is shared evenly between the fasteners before
one of them fails, of which the transfer block showed clear rotation once the ultimate strength
was reached, see Figure 10.13. A selection of graphs from the C1 series are presented below,
see Figure 10.11as they represent the recordings of the failure at the location of the LVDT.

Test series L1
For test series L1 it was observed that the failure was more evenly shared between the screws,
resulting in the final deformation to be more uniform compared to the CLT test series. This is
assumed to come from the homogenized structure of the LVL where the withdrawal strength
is more likely to be similar for both screws. The results showed a predictable outcome with
a low coefficient of variation, see Table 10.15. The failure was slow and showed relatively
high residual capacity in 5/6 cases as can be observed in Figure 10.14.

10.4 Test results
Friction contribution
The friction contribution was estimated on the basis of the two test series performed in LVL,
L1 and L2NF. As the friction of the Teflon was measured and thereby known, the friction con-
tribution of steel to wood for L1 could be calculated assuming that the withdrawal strength
was similar given the equal conditions. The withdrawal strength, fax was adjusted so that the
measured characteristic capacity would match that of the truss model. Given that the friction
coefficient was set to µ = 0.19 in the model, yielded a withdrawal strength of fax = 7.86MPa
which is relatively high but not unlikely for LVL. The applied calculation model for LVL is
the one presented in ETA-11/0030, which uses the insertion length minus one diameter as the
effective anchoring length. The Friction coefficient was on this basis found to be µest = 0.52,
which is relatively high, but possible. For detailed calculations of the determination of the
friction coefficient see Appendix B.9.
The estimated friction coefficient µexp = 0.52 was then applied to the calculation model
for the CLT, to see if the high friction coefficient could predict the experimental results.
The withdrawal strength, being the second model parameter, was determined according the
model by Ringhofer et al.(2015), with corrected densities ρref,k = 440kg/m3 and mean
density ρmean = 468kg/m3, ref. Table 10.2. The withdrawal strength was then found to
be fax = 5.96MPa, while the along grain capacity for the individual fastener was found to
be Fv.R = 40.77kN, compared to the characteristic measured strength of Fv.R.exp = 42.4kN,
which is only a 4% deviation.
Adjusting the withdrawal strength to fax = 6.2MPa and maintaining the friction coefficient
at 0.52 retrieves the measured capacity of Fv.R.exp = 42.4kN. The deviation in capacity may
originate from a dowel contribution as the longer screws in CLT had some minor noticeable
bending deformation. Regarding the high value obtained for the friction coefficient, a sig-
nificant friction contribution of 34% of the full capacity was calculated. This is noticeably
grater than the current friction coefficient of 0.25 by to-days standards.
When comparing the measured strength with the calculated capacity in accordance with the
current standards and technical approvals it becomes evident that there is a large capacity po-
tential. As an example, Rothoblaas presents an along grain capacity for the 11x225 fastener
in CLT/Glulam at 19.15kN, which is increased to 23.94kN with the inclusion of friction
contribution using µ = 0.25. The value of 19.15kN is based on the calculation method in
the ETA-11/0030 which, by adjusting for the actual sample lot and using ρk = 440kg/m3,
yields an increased along grain capacity of 21.31kN which including friction gives 26.6kN.
That results in a modest 62% of the characteristic capacity that was measure with a mag-
nitude of Fv.R.est = 42.4kN, showing that both the friction contribution and the withdrawal
strength is under predicted.

==PAGE:116==
10 Experimental testing of Inclined screws with steel plates as outer members
Stiffness models
The second objective of the testing was to determine the along grain or parallel to force stiff-
ness of inclined screws and to compare it with the available stiffness models. Two stiffness
models have been used in this comparison, the projected linear spring model and the model
presented in prEN 2020, see chapter 3 for details.
The results show that there was not a significant difference in the stiffness between test series
L1 and L2NF, which can be interpreted that the friction does not appear to have a significant
impact on the stiffness.
The linear projected spring model uses the withdrawal stiffness equation from the ETA-
11/0030 where the effective anchoring length is determined by the threaded part minus one
diameter, similar to the method presented by Pirnbacher (2009). The stiffness of L1 com-
pared to that of the linear projected spring model (LSM), and the prEN 2020 are presented
in Eq.(10.1). The friction coefficient in the prEN method is corrected to µ = 0.52.

11.16
Direct comparison
In Figure 10.20 the two sets of graphs have been plotted together to highlight the differences.
The difference between dowel type fasteners loaded perpendicular or in their axis becomes
quite obvious. It should be noted that the perpendicular screws have a slightly smaller nom-
inal diameter at 10mm vs. 11mm. That said, the core diameter is similar for both screws
due to their geometry, which is where the perpendicular inserted screw gains most of its
resistance.
The stiffness of the inclined screws are noticeably higher, approximately 5 times. In addition,
the inclined screws gain strength almost immediately, with little to no slip due to the way
the screws are installed. The perpendicular screws require a large permanent deformation
to gain full ultimate strength, which is mostly permanent. The behaviour is more ductile as
parts of the deformation comes from the plastic behaviour of the steel core.

10.6
discussion
The accuracy of the data are always a concern. The friction coefficient was measured to be
noticeably high at around 0.5. Additional measurements of the friction coefficient could have
been performed to ensure that the friction coefficient behaved linear for increased magnitude
of load.
A contributing factor to the high withdrawal capacity of the wood is the fact that the CLT
specimens were of sound quality with little defects, cracks or gaps, and had generally high
density. It would also be complimentary to perform withdrawal tests of the wood samples
at 45◦using the same fasteners to have a direct value for the input parameter of the truss
model. This way the only unknown factor would be the friction contribution, assuming that
the model is representative for the kinematic behaviour.
Other factors that could affect the results are related to human errors or the installation pro-
cedure. In some cases the steel plates would, due to the screw angle, end up at slightly
different elevations in reference to the load transfer block. Although the load transfer block
109
==PAGE:122==
10 Experimental testing of Inclined screws with steel plates as outer members
was ”hinged” this may have lead to one side attracting a higher portion of the load. In fu-
ture testing it may be advised to use an additional perpendicular screw during installation
to restrain the steel plate while torquing the screws, and thus ensure that the load transfer
block is level at load initiation. The video footage showed that the load transfer block was
relatively horizontal during the testing, which can be interpreted as to the fasteners sharing
the load relatively evenly until failure. The deviations of the test results might as well come
from variations of the wood specimens.
The eccentricity of the single screw may also contribute to the results. The screws where
offset equally at both sides so that the thrust line would be centric in the steel plate. Having
two or more pairs of screws would even out this effect. A possible reason for the high friction
contribution that was measured could partially originate from the spatial bulging around the
washers. This permanent deformation ensures contact pressure around the fasteners between
wood and steel. A similar behaviour is expected for larger connections with multiple fasten-
ers. It should be noted that the friction was measured for bear steel to wood, and any type of
surface coating like galvanization may yiled a different outcome.
All of the tests were govern by screw withdrawal failure and the screws showed minor bend-
ing deformations. The bearing effects is assumed to be negligible as the total along wood
axis deflection was in the range of 4mm −5mm. Other factors that could affect the mea-
surements were the accuracy of LVDT reading from the hardwood platou.
10.7
Conclusion
The test results of this study showed that the load capacity of inclined screws with steel plates
as outer members were high, even higher than predicted. The stiffness of the connection is
also impressively high compared to perpendicular fasteners, about 5 times, with little to no
initial slip. The friction contribution was estimated to be around 34% with a friction co-
efficient of µ = 0.52. After the friction and input parameters were corrected for the actual
samples the truss model showed good correlation between obtained and calculated charac-
teristic values. Based on this, the model was found to be a reliable and an efficient way of
designing these types of joints.
The variation of stiffness from the results was significantly higher than the variation of the
capacity, however, the estimated stiffness was in both cases in the range of the measured,
and can therefore be assessed as satisfactory in this case. The model in prEN2020 did show
a better fit for the initial linear range k1, once corrected for the high fiction coefficient and
measured mean density, while the linear projected spring model was better at capturing the
second linear elastic range k2.
For structural detailing it is suggested that the diameter to effective threaded insertion length
is determined so that head tear off is not a possible failure mode, as this mode yields no
residual capacity. Depending on the yield strength of the fastener and the withdrawal strength
of the wood, this ratio could be in the range of 16 - 20d. For symmetrical tension joints,
the screws should also cross each other in the axis of the timber member by at least 4d as
suggested by Krenn & Schickhofer (2009) to avoid tension failure perpendicular to the grain.
110
==PAGE:123==
CHAPTER11
Conclusion and Reflection
11.1
Conclusion
The investigations made in this study show that it is possible to build superstructures of up to
five storeys entirely of Mass Timber within reasonable dimensions and structural complexity.
By orienting the CLT panels vertically and joining multiple panels together with sufficient
stiffness allows for high in-plane strength and stiffness, making the walls capable of with-
standing the lateral effects from wind. The use of high strength - high stiffness base hold
down connections increases the resistance to lateral deformation fulfilling the displacement
criteria in serviceability limit state. Moreover, using inclined screws loaded in the screws
axis have also proven to be a high capacity - high stiffness alternative to conventional dowel
type fasteners with capacity exceeding the predicted models.
By using existing dividing walls as the bracing system there is little additional cost involved
in bracing the structure as these walls would normally be present. A selection of joining
techniques that are simple to manufacture and install aids this structural bracing system to be
competitive in cost and installation compared to the material competitors, steel and concrete.
The solutions presented in this study show promising results for low to medium rise struc-
tures. Preliminary design showed that it is challenging to increase the number of floors by
a significant amount as the overturning moment and wind load magnitude increases rapidly
with height for the given floor layout. By taking advantage of using a less conservative force
distribution, the building could house another level without significant changes. However,
for a structure taller than 6 storeys, having the same storey height, modifications would have
to be made for the proposed wall thickness or possibly layout. Additional wood trusses would
be needed in the facade if the building were to exceed 6 storeys.
11.2
Reflection
The presented base hold down connection has concerns regarding its ductility, especially for
seismic loads. Although it is possible to develop a stiff connection with a weak ductile link,
this is often more challenging to obtain in reality than on the drawing board. This is mainly
from the deviation of the real strength compared to the declared design strength.
As the base hold down connection is designed for a particular scenario in which the favourable
stabilising self weight of the structure is not fully present, the actual load in the connection
at ultimate limit state for the lifespan of the structure will therefore be noticeably lower than
the design load due to the favourable self weight introduced after this particular scenario.
That may not necessarily be the case for seismic loading where the additional mass is what
leads to the forces in the system set in motion.
The connection designs that have been presented are subject to further development and
investigation for cyclic loading, earthquake and in particular fire design. This may be per-
==PAGE:124==
11 Conclusion and Reflection
formed by detailed numerical analysis or experimental testing.

APPENDIXA
Example 1: Determination of
load bearing wall from
design diagram
A continuous balloon-frame type wall in a commercial building is loaded according to Fig-
ure A.1a. The moment and axial force diagram given by the software is presented in Fig-
ure A.1a c) and d). The load is of duration medium term (m), with kmod = 0.8. The wall is
supported at each elevation by the floor diaphragm and the buckling is assumed to follow a
sinusoidal shape with buckling length le = 3.8m. The floor slabs are placed on a wall corbel
with eccentricity e = 200mm. It is sought to determine the dimensions for the load-bearing
wall for the initial analysis.
(a) a) Statical system, b) assumed buckling shape,
c) design moment, d) design axial force
(b) Capacity diagram for combined N + M section forces.
Figure A.1 a) Statical system, b) capacity curve for CLT 140mm wall
The worst combination of moment and axial load is found by the static analysis to be Md =
8.9 kNm and Nd = 320 kN. Reading the diagram for a 140mm CLT wall with buckling length
of 3.8m yields a capacity of Md = 10 kNm and Nd = 500 kN i.e. the wall has sufficient
capacity. The values are only applicable for ultimate limit state, hence serviceability- or
accidental limit state has to be verified in the final design.

Withdrawal strength and withdrawal capacity of inclined

==PAGE:131==
Joints with inclined screws and and steel plates as outer members
Dowel type fasteners carrying in bending/shear are limited by embedment strength and bending capacity of 
fastener. This yields a low stiffness and load carrying capacity compared to the tensile and withdrawel capacity 
of the same fastener. Inclined screws (45deg) have shown to have greate stength due to their withdrawel 
capacity. Larger connections in CLT panels are preferably mounted on the face of the panel as it is challenging 
and time consuming to slot out larger hidden steel plates which are more common for wood trusses. 
Truss model
Krenn & Schickhofer (2009) proposed a truss model which is simple to implement when evaluating the 
capacity of the connection. The small displacement in the shear plane (3-4mm) indicated that the dowel 
effect can be neglected. The friction contribution from the inclination should be accounted for as it will 
develope as soon as the joint is loaded. This is especially the case for thinner steel plates with low out-of-
plane bending stiffness. The proposed friction coefficiten

0.32

prefered design model based on its simplicity. The abbreviations of the moel are described in the following.

==PAGE:139==
B.2 Equivalent spring stiffness for inclined fasteners
B.2
Equivalent spring stiffness for inclined fasteners
The Schemeatics show a screw inserted at an angle α to the system axis.
Figure B.1 Equivalent along axis spring stiffness keq for a screw inserted at an angle to the system axis.
The angles are exaggerated to highlight the trigonometry. Assuming small angel change. The
hollow circle refers to the initial position.
Force equilibrium in the direction of the system axis is described such that a compression
of the spring is negative by sign convention. For the purpose of the derivation it does not
matter if the spring is compressed or elongated.
Rax = −k ·u cosα
(B.1)
where u is a displacement in the horizontal direction and k is the along force spring stiffness.
The force component of Rax in the lateral direction, Rx, is determined from
Rx = Rax ·cosα
(B.2)
Now substituting (B.1) into (B.2) gives the expression
Rx = (−k ·u·cosα)·cosα
Rx = −k ·u·cos2 α
(B.3)
Saying that there is an equivalent spring force in the lateral direction
Rx = −keq ·u
(B.4)
And then setting the two expressions of Rx equal to each other, (B.3) = (B.4) the expression
becomes
−keq ·u = −k ·u cosα
(B.5)
keq = k ·cos2 α
(B.6)

==PAGE:146==
Loads
Loads acting on the shear wall are the following:
Wind
Structural dead load, CLT slabs / walls
Imposed dead load (materials, topping etc)
Imposed load category B. office 
Imposed load is set to 3.0 kN/m2 to provide some 
flexibility 
No reduction factor for multiple levels of office. 
Loads denoted with subscript a, refer to adjacent. Adjacent loads are introduced as point loads from 
beams connecting to the narrow face of the CLT wall. Their magnitude will be determined in the following 
based on the contributary area loading the beam.
Due to the complexity of the static system the loads will be introduced to a FEM model, with respective 
load cases and load combinations.

between slab and CLT wall. Two methods of supporting the slabs are presented below. Regardless of the 
choice the eccentric behaviour becomes more or less the same.

Out of plane moment
out of plane moments are calculated for one case to 
verify the FEM model. Most critical situation will occure 
for either LC2 or LC3, which results in a combined out 
of plane bending and buckling. LC3 will be applied as 
this gives the largest out of plane moments.
The loads are applied according to the figure to the 
right. Self weight only on one side (reduced load) and 
self weigt + imposed load on the other side.

B Calculations
142
==PAGE:155==
Results From FEM-Design
Stress plots 
LC1: sigma x' top 
The highest axial stresses arises from Load combination LC1. When investigating the axial tension and 
compressive stresses it should be kept in mind that boundry conditions and point loads may introduce
singularities. This is especially the case for the modeled hold down, which in its real form transferes loads 
over a surface and not simply by a line. The high local values in red seen above mainly arise from this 
inaccuracy. The base hold down is designed to have transfered the total force over iths height of about 
650mm. When verifying the gross tensile capacity the more accurate location to investigate is around this 
height, in which the full strength of the connection is gained.
For the compressive stresses on the other hand it is seen that the maximum is local in 
the bottom right corner. This will possibly crush a small part of the corner which is not 
critical for the shear wall being naturally stable, see fig to right. Any buckling of the wall 
will occure around the center part of the free vertical height. It should be noted that the 
wall is supported along its outer edges by the other perpendicular shear walls which will 
increase the resistance to buckling. The buckling stress is therefore extracted from the 
middle height of the storey height. 
When investigating the tensile and compressive stresses a group of critical points are assesed. The points 
are shown below. 
B.4 Verification of critical shear wall

Simplifying the FEM model (2D problem)
Now that it has been determined which load combination are the most critical the model is simplified to 
neglect out of plane behaviour as this showed to have negligeble effect.
In order to avoid singularities as much as possible the wind load is altered from a single point load to a 
line load acting along the walls length.

B.4 Verification of critical shear wall
149
==PAGE:162==
Shear stress benchmarking
The complex model is showing signs of singularities from the simplifications in modelling. A simplified 
model have been created in order to make an effort to benchmark the results. The simple model is a 4m 
tall wall loaded by a horisontal shear force along the wall lenght with a magnitude of 94.7 kN/m, which is 
equal to the full magnitude of the lateral wind load.

Comparison with FEM-Design
The same model (same spring stiffnesses) is calculated in FEM-design. Unlike the handcalculation FEM-Design 
uses the more sophisticated in-plane stiffness of the CLT elements. It is possible to some degree to evaluate 
the individual contributions by modelling and running the FE analysis individually.
Result from FEM-Design for characteristic wind only (mm) - non linear uplift
Note: 
The FEM model is resisting sliding by means of friction in the compression zone. This leads to a difference of 
about 2mm between the hand calculations and the FEM model.

(superposition). Characteristic values are found using a factor of 0.84 to mathc with FEM-Design

Stiffness relation wood-steel connection
The wood has to be able to transfer the loads in tension to the steel plate. Only the lamellae oriented along 
the height is effective due to the stiffness along the grain. The stiffness relation between the wood and the 
steel plate affects how the load is distributed. It is therefore suggested to find a suitable relation that gives 
similar stiffness of both parts.

Several possible failure mechanisms have to be investigated to ensure the structural integrity of the 
connection. These are the following:

f) Steel plates net
g) Steel plates (steel - concrete)

Stiffness of the Base Hold Down connection
Experimental test campaigns initiated by Rothoblaas have shown that fastener groups in CLT and glulam may 
have up to half the theorethical stiffness (catalogue). There are uncertainties to how much stiffness can be 
expected and thus a range of stiffnesses are used. 
Krenn & Schickhofer (2009) found that the stiffness of inclined screws in serviceability state could be 
estimated using n to a power of 0.8

to be assesed.

Krenn & Schickhofer reported that a single screw of dimensions 8x200mm had a stiffness of 30kN/mm, in a

measured in the screws axis or along the system axis.
Combined stiffness of the connection
The joint stiffness of the connection is determined by the individual contributions from; composite action 
(threaded screw-wood), the elongation of the steel plate, deformation of the bearing around the washers, 
friction between stee-wood and possibly more depending on the final design of the connector. The stiffness 
can be visualized as a set of linear springs all with individual stiffnesses. The use of superposition, assuming 
deformation in the elastic range, results in the following total equivalent spring stiffness.

B.7 Base Hold Down connection
171
==PAGE:184==
e) Block shearing/ group tear out
The capacity of the connection has to be verified for block shearing / group tear-out. The method given in the 
national Annex A of DS/EN 1995-1-1 will be used. 
fig. source: J. Munch-Andersen
Based on the geometry of the connection with screws from both sides the block failure is the only possible 
failure mode. If the plate where nailed from both sides plug shearing would also be possible.
CLT elements have additional transverse reinforcement from the orthogonal layers. These layers contribute to 
prevent block tear out by taking shear forces across the fibres and redistributing them. Alternatively, the blue 
dashed failure-line may be verified for shearing of the transverse boards. Brandner et al. (2013) showed that 
the net strength of CLT is higher than for solid timber due to this reinforcing effect. 
DS/EN 1995-1-1 national Annex A
The geometry of the connection gives the following possible failure lines

B.7 Base Hold Down connection
173
==PAGE:186==
a2) Tension in wood - FE analysis
The load transition from the steel to the wood is gradual due to the stiffness and length of the connection. So 
far the geometry of the connection is govenr by tension failure, gross or block failure. It is sought to reduced 
the width of the connection to take advantage of the larger internal moment arm. 
The base hold down connection is modelled as linear spring supports for each row of inclined screws.
Screw diameter
≔
d

Vertical Joint
The vertical joint between the panels has to have sufficient capacity and stiffness. The stiffness of the joint 
plays a role in the kinematic behaviour of the wall. To achieve the desired kinematic behaviour, and thus 
maintain the design load in the base hold down the wall must achieve single panel behaviour, more on this 
later. Different designs will be investigate to see if they are capable of transfering the forces. Factors like 
manufacturing and assemblyy will also be evaluated as they are important to the practical design.

B.8 Vertical joint connection
181
==PAGE:194==
Shear flow
Before attending to estimate the amount of fasteners needed for the joint the magnitude of the shear forces 
has to be determined. The magnitude of the shear flow depends on i.e. the kinematic behaviour of the wall, 
stiffness of the vertical joint and boundry conditions. A benchmarking example will be presented in the 
followign to proove the concept and aid to verfiry the outcome of the numerical investigation to come later.  
Three cases are presented in the figure below. The fully rigid case (a) having the highest stiffness will take the 
ordinary parabgolic shape. The fully seperated case (b) will have less resistance to deformation and take the 
shape of two independent parabolas. The intermediate case (c) having a joint stiffness will land somewhere 
between the two first extreemes. What is important to note is the magnitude of shear flow the intermediate 
case can attract. Since the shearflow is a function of the stiffness of the joint, a less stiff joint will also attract 
less shear forces.
a) Fully rigid, 
b) fully seperated, 
c) intermediate with vertical stiffness  kv = 30 000kN/m
deformations and shear flow 
for three cases with varying joint stiffness.
nxy
The deformations are solely from the laterall wind load. From experience the stiffness of the vertical joint is 
usually around 30 - 50 000 kN/m. 
B Calculations
182
==PAGE:195==
It should be noted that the cantilevered beam model is mainly applicable when the h/b ratio is 4 or more. In 
this case the ratio is 3.3 which classifies it as a tall beam, which takes the form of non-linear stress 
distribution. The FEM solver FEM-Design is thereofre used to determine the exact shear flow. Beam theory is 
therefore only used to compare results.  
ref. CLT -design and 
use, Henrik Danielsson,
Lund University
Approximate shear flow in vertical joint
As the process of investigating the vertical joint is iterative the approximate joint forces presented below are 
are based on three load cases that will be investigated more in detail later. As the forces vary with stiffness 
to cases with high stiffness k = 50- and k = 80 000kN/m are run to give an estimate shear flow in the joint for 
sizing and spacing the fasteners.

Half lap joint with 90 degree screws.

these type of fasteners perform poorly to clamp the panels together. This is easily achieved with a 
smoothed shank screw like the HBS type fastener, which paired with a washer shows great increase in 
capacity. The joint is initially investigated with 90 degree fastener orientation to the face of the CLT as any 
angular approach becomes cumbersome on site.

To obtain proper bearing the shear key can be placed in two ways as shown below. This introduces varying 
degrees of eccentrivity to the joint. The thickness of the panel allows for innstallation from both sides with 
the middle board intact as a positive stop. Twosided innstalation will increase the capacity, but can lead to 
problems on site with access from both sides. It is desirable/ only possible to install the shear keys from one

B Calculations
188
==PAGE:201==
Alternative 3 Shear keys of wood.
Though having high capacity and good shear transfere this type of connection complicates manufactur ing to 
a high degree. The shear keys will require tight tollerances which combined with moisture variation may 
cause problems on site.
Proposals have been made to use some sort of bearing compound in the joint which would eradicate 
tolleances by leaving a gap of 10-15mm between the panels. Two options are presented below showing.
a) direct bearing
b) 10-15mm gap to later be filled with a 
compound. 
In case a) with direct bearing, gaps will most likely form. For case b) this issue is removed by introducing a 
filler. Introducing liquids that later harden is considered a wet-solution and is not prefered in terms of 
deconstruction at end of lifecycle. 
Further investigation is required for this joiunt to realise the potential benefits. One of the major 
shortcomings is the aditional CNC work needed. With that said the innstalation imay be fast as long as 
the tollereances are not to restrictive. 
No verification is performed for this joint at this stage. 
B.8 Vertical joint connection
189
==PAGE:202==
Alternative 4 Internal plate of metal (steel aluminium alloy)
A high capacity shear connection where the steel plate is imbedded in the centre of the clt with a routed 
grove in both elements. The slotted groove could be installed in one of the elements prior to transport 
reducing the amount of work on site. 
This connector can be performed with dowels, or self tapping dowels in the case 
of aluminium alloy. Self tapping dowels will be investigated as it is challenging to 
perform long joints with tight tollerance dowels. Due to the orientation of the 
lamellas the lower embedment strength (90deg) of dowel action is used as the 
primary dowel effect will occure in the transverse layers.

B.8 Vertical joint connection

==PAGE:206==
Alternative lap/spline plates of wood
An alternative to the metal plate is to use LVL or another wood 
based product.
The wood based material would have to carry the shear force in its 
net section. A single lap plate will have to carry the shear force, 
which has been seen in the calculation  of the halflap is on the 
breach of capacity. 
double lap plate
Shortcomings are amongst others less stiffness from the 
wood- wood connection and larger slip. A double lap 
plate require tightly spaced screws, while a single lap 
joint may use self borting dowels or similar. 
Due to these shortcomings the connections have not been 
investigated further. 
Single lap plate
B Calculations
194
==PAGE:207==
Numerical investigation of coupling behaviour
The kinematic behaviour of the adjacent walls is paramount to ensure the desired load transfere. In the 
case of coupled wall behaviour the internal moment arm is drasticly reduced leading to higher tension 
forces in the base hold down. Rothoblaas reports through their test campaign that at equal condition s the 
stiffness ratio kv / kh determines the rotational behaviour of the wall. 
This being said ther are no quantified method that states when the wall goes from coupled to single
behaviour. Many parameters effect this kinematic behaviour such as the walls ratio, vertical joint length, 
stiffness of hold down, vertical load etc. Numerical modelling is therefore used to determine the behaviour 
boundries of this spesific case. A more desireable outcome woyuld be to have a general solution that cover 
several situations.  
For the numerical investigation the followign parameters are set

When the wall lifts in the connection qthe shear forces are taken by the joint and no contact forces are

slightly. The added self weight pushes the wall to equilibrium at its normal unloaded state and thus also 
induces contact pressure in the edge of the left panel. Note that the anchor force is reduced and th e prying 
effects does not lead to a critical state for the base hold down. It can be further stated that any increase in 
the joint stiffness will lea to single behaviour.

==PAGE:215==
Analysis of connection forces of the joint
The shear forces that are able to develope in the vertical joint depend on the stiffness of the connection. If 
the wall has the same stiffness throughout and behaved like a vertical beam the shear flow would take the 
shape 
＝
nxy
⋅
―
3
2
―
V
A
In this case hte vertical beam consist of panels connected to each other with elastic springs (fasteners). Some 
slip will therefore take place in the joint between the panels and thus the transverse shear forces can not 
develope 100%. This results in an iterative process where the needed amount of fasteners are reduced due to 
the lack of forces being transfered in the joint. This may help engineers to design more manageble 
connections with less connectors. Once the new spacing of fasteners are determined the stiffness will vary 
leading to a new FEM iteration. It is thereofre suggested to use an high initial stiffness to attract sufficient 
amounts of forces. 
Numerical analysis in FEM-Design

B.9 Capacity and stiffness, experimental vs. analytical
213
==PAGE:226==
Stiffness of the connection - CLT
The aim of this calculation is to determine a suitable stiffness model that aligns with the results from the 
experimental testing
The deformation contribution from the steel plates and bearing are not accounted for and will lead to a 
higher overall displacement and thus lower stiffness.

APPENDIXC
C.1
Experimental testing
All plots for test series C1 can be seen below. In test C1.1 - C1.3 the LVDT was recorded
moving in the screws bit slot, which is why the deflections show strange or unexpected be-
haviour. In C1.7 the LVDT fell out of its position. The readings until this stage were still fine.
For tests C1.10 the contraption holding the LVDT binded and moved with the steel plates
due to the spatial bulging and plate deformation. This was corrected and did not occur in
any later tests.
